There\xe2\x80\x99s no harm in telling him that,\xe2\x80\x9d the young man went on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI advise you not to tell it to Mademoiselle; she\xe2\x80\x99s quite in the old\r\nideas,\xe2\x80\x9d Madame Poupin suggested to Hyacinth, tasting her _tisane_.\r\n\r\nHyacinth sat baffled and wondering, looking from his fellow-labourer in\r\nSoho to his new acquaintance opposite. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you\xe2\x80\x99ve some plan, something\r\nto which one can give one\xe2\x80\x99s self, I think you might have told me,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nremarked in a moment to Poupin.\r\n\r\nThe latter merely viewed him a little as if he were a pleasing object\r\nand then said to the strange young man: \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s a little jealous of you.\r\nBut there\xe2\x80\x99s no harm in that; it\xe2\x80\x99s of his age. You must know him, you\r\nmust like him. We\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you his history some other day; it will make\r\nyou feel that he belongs to us of necessity. It\xe2\x80\x99s an accident that he\r\nhasn\xe2\x80\x99t met you here before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow could _ces messieurs_ have met when M. Paul never comes? He\r\ndoesn\xe2\x80\x99t spoil us!\xe2\x80\x9d Madame Poupin cried.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you see I\xe2\x80\x99ve my little sister at home to take care of when I\r\nain\xe2\x80\x99t at the works,\xe2\x80\x9d M. It will be a\r\ntremendous job and only the strong will prevail,\xe2\x80\x9d his host murmured\r\na little wearily, turning his eyes to Madame Poupin, who approached\r\nslowly, holding the _tisane_ in rather a full bowl and tasting it again\r\nand yet again as she came.\r\n\r\nHyacinth had been watching his fellow-visitor with deepening interest;\r\na fact of which M. I\xe2\x80\x99m sure I\xe2\x80\x99ve nothing against it. I like to\r\nknow folk if they\xe2\x80\x99re likely to be worth it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHyacinth was too pleased with this even to take it up; it seemed to him\r\nfor a moment that he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t touch it gracefully enough. But he said\r\nwith sufficient eagerness: \xe2\x80\x9cWill you tell me all about your plot?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it\xe2\x80\x99s no plot. Madame Poupin announced\r\nas much, with the addition that if the young men wished to make\r\nacquaintance they must do it outside; their friend must be perfectly\r\nquiet. They accordingly withdrew with apologies and promises to return\r\nfor further news on the morrow, and two minutes later Hyacinth found\r\nhimself standing face to face with his companion on the pavement in\r\nfront of M. My\r\nname _is_ Paul, though\xe2\x80\x94Paul Muniment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what\xe2\x80\x99s your trade?\xe2\x80\x9d Hyacinth demanded with a jump into\r\nfamiliarity; for his friend seemed to have told him a great deal more\r\nthan was usually conveyed in that item of information.\r\n\r\nPaul Muniment looked down at him from above broad shoulders. \xe2\x80\x9cI work\r\nfor a firm of wholesale chemists at Lambeth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd where do you live?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI live over the water too; in the far south of London.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd are you going home now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes, I\xe2\x80\x99m going to toddle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd may I toddle with you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Muniment considered him further and then gave a laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll carry\r\nyou if you like.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you; I expect I can walk as far as you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hyacinth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I admire your spirit and I daresay I shall like your company.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was something in his face, taken in connexion with the idea\r\nthat he was concerned in the taking of a stand\xe2\x80\x94it offered our quick\r\nyouth the image of a rank of bristling bayonets\xe2\x80\x94which made Hyacinth\r\nfeel the desire to go with him till he dropped; and in a moment they\r\nstarted away together and took the direction Muniment had mentioned.\r\nThey discoursed as they went, exchanging a great many opinions and\r\nanecdotes; but they reached the south-westerly court in which the\r\nyoung chemist lived with his infirm sister before he had told Hyacinth\r\nanything definite about the \xe2\x80\x9cpoints\xe2\x80\x9d of his reference or Hyacinth,\r\non his side, had detailed the circumstances involved in his being,\r\naccording to M. Poupin, one of the disinherited. Hyacinth didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nwish to press, wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t for the world have appeared indiscreet, and\r\nmoreover, though he had taken so great a fancy to Muniment, was not\r\nquite prepared as yet to be pressed himself. Therefore it failed to\r\nbecome very clear how his companion had made Poupin\xe2\x80\x99s acquaintance and\r\nhow long he had enjoyed it. Paul Muniment nevertheless was to a certain\r\nextent communicative, especially on the question of his living in a\r\nvery poor little corner. He had his sister to keep\xe2\x80\x94she could do nothing\r\nfor herself; and he paid a low rent because she had to have doctors and\r\ndoses and all sorts of little comforts. He spent a bob a week for her\r\non flowers. It was better too when you got upstairs, and from the back\r\nwindows you could see the dome of Saint Paul\xe2\x80\x99s. Audley Court, with its\r\npretty name, which reminded Hyacinth of Tennyson, proved to be a still\r\ndingier nook than Lomax Place; and it had the further drawback that you\r\nhad to penetrate a narrow alley, a passage between high black walls,\r\nto enter it. At the door of one of the houses the young men paused,\r\nlingering a little, and then Muniment said: \xe2\x80\x9cI say, why shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t you\r\ncome up? I like you well enough for that, and you can see my sister;\r\nher name\xe2\x80\x99s Rosy.\xe2\x80\x9d He spoke as if this would be a great privilege and\r\nadded, for the joke, that Rosy enjoyed a call from a gentleman of\r\nall things. Hyacinth needed no urging, and he groped his way at his\r\ncompanion\xe2\x80\x99s heels up a dark staircase which appeared to him\xe2\x80\x94for they\r\nstopped only when they could go no further\xe2\x80\x94the longest and steepest he\r\nhad ever ascended. At the top Paul Muniment pushed open a door, but\r\nexclaimed \xe2\x80\x9cHullo, have you gone to roost?\xe2\x80\x9d on perceiving the room on\r\nthe threshold of which they stood to be unlighted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh dear, no; we\xe2\x80\x99re sitting in the dark,\xe2\x80\x9d a small bright voice\r\ninstantly replied. \xe2\x80\x9cLady Aurora\xe2\x80\x99s so kind; she\xe2\x80\x99s here still.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe voice came out of a corner so pervaded by gloom that the speaker\r\nwas indistinguishable. \xe2\x80\x9cWell now, that\xe2\x80\x99s beautiful!\xe2\x80\x9d Paul Muniment\r\nrejoined. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll have a party then, for I\xe2\x80\x99ve brought some one else.\r\nWe\xe2\x80\x99re poor, you know, but honest, and not afraid of showing up, and I\r\ndaresay we can manage a candle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt this, in the dim firelight, Hyacinth saw a tall figure erect\r\nitself\xe2\x80\x94a figure angular and slim, crowned with a large vague hat and\r\na flowing umbrageous veil. This unknown person gave a singular laugh\r\nand said: \xe2\x80\x9cOh I brought some candles; we could have had a light if we\r\nhad wished.\xe2\x80\x9d Both the tone and the purport of the words announced to\r\nHyacinth that they proceeded from Lady Aurora.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nVIII\r\n\r\n\r\nPaul Muniment took a match out of his pocket and lighted it on the sole\r\nof his shoe; after which he applied it to a tallow candle which stood\r\nin a tin receptacle on the low mantel-shelf. This enabled Hyacinth\r\nto perceive a narrow bed in a corner and a small object stretched\r\nupon it\xe2\x80\x94an object revealed to him mainly by the bright fixedness of a\r\npair of large eyes, of which the whites were sharply contrasted with\r\nthe dark pupil and which gazed at him across a counterpane of gaudy\r\npatchwork. The brown room seemed crowded with heterogeneous objects\r\nand presented moreover, thanks to a multitude of small prints, both\r\nplain and coloured, fastened all over the walls, a highly-decorated\r\nappearance. The little person in the corner had the air of having gone\r\nto bed in a picture-gallery, and as soon as Hyacinth became aware of\r\nthis his impression deepened that Paul Muniment and his sister were\r\nvery wonderful people. Lady Aurora hovered before him with an odd\r\ndrooping, swaying erectness, and she laughed a good deal, vaguely and\r\nshyly, as for the awkwardness of her being found still on the premises.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRosy, girl, I\xe2\x80\x99ve brought you a visitor,\xe2\x80\x9d Hyacinth\xe2\x80\x99s guide soon said.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis young man has walked all the way from Lisson Grove to make your\r\nacquaintance.\xe2\x80\x9d Rosy continued to look at the visitor from over her\r\ncounterpane, and he felt slightly embarrassed, for he had never yet\r\nbeen presented to a young lady in her position. \xe2\x80\x9cYou mustn\xe2\x80\x99t mind her\r\nbeing in bed\xe2\x80\x94she\xe2\x80\x99s always in bed,\xe2\x80\x9d her brother went on. \xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s in bed\r\njust the same as a little slippery trout\xe2\x80\x99s in the water.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear me, if I didn\xe2\x80\x99t receive company because I was in bed, there\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be much use, would there, Lady Aurora?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRosy put this question in a light, gay tone, with a dart of shining\r\neyes at her companion, who replied at once with still greater hilarity\r\nand in a voice which struck Hyacinth as strange and affected: \xe2\x80\x9cOh\r\nmercy, no; it seems quite the natural place!\xe2\x80\x9d Then she added: \xe2\x80\x9cAnd it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsuch a lovely bed, such a comfortable bed!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed it is, when your ladyship makes it up,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rosy; while\r\nHyacinth wondered at this strange phenomenon of a peer\xe2\x80\x99s daughter (for\r\nhe knew she must be that) performing the functions of a housemaid.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say now, you haven\xe2\x80\x99t been doing that again to-day?\xe2\x80\x9d Muniment asked,\r\npunching the mattress of the invalid with a vigorous hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPray, who would if I didn\xe2\x80\x99t?\xe2\x80\x9d Lady Aurora inquired. \xe2\x80\x9cIt only takes a\r\nminute if one knows how.\xe2\x80\x9d Her manner was jocosely apologetic and she\r\nseemed to plead guilty to having been absurd; in the dim light Hyacinth\r\nthought he saw her blush as if she were much embarrassed. In spite of\r\nher blushing her appearance and manner suggested to him a personage in\r\na comedy. She sounded the letter _r_ as a _w_.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can do it beautifully. I often do it, when Mrs. Major doesn\xe2\x80\x99t come\r\nup,\xe2\x80\x9d Paul Muniment said, continuing to thump his sister\xe2\x80\x99s couch in an\r\nappreciative but somewhat subversive manner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I\xe2\x80\x99ve no doubt whatever!\xe2\x80\x9d Lady Aurora exclaimed quickly. \xe2\x80\x9cMrs.\r\nMajor must have so very much to do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot in the making-up of beds, I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid; there are only two or three,\r\ndown there, for so many,\xe2\x80\x9d the young man returned loudly and with a kind\r\nof inconsequent cheerfulness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I\xe2\x80\x99ve thought a great deal about that. But there wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be room\r\nfor more, you know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lady Aurora, this time with all gravity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s not much room for a family of that sort anywhere\xe2\x80\x94thirteen\r\npeople of all ages and sizes,\xe2\x80\x9d her host observed. \xe2\x80\x9cThe world\xe2\x80\x99s pretty\r\nbig, but there doesn\xe2\x80\x99t seem room.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re also thirteen at home,\xe2\x80\x9d Lady Aurora hastened to mention. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nalso rather crowded.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSurely you don\xe2\x80\x99t mean at Inglefield?\xe2\x80\x9d Rosy demanded from her dusky\r\nnook.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know about Inglefield. I\xe2\x80\x99m so much in town.\xe2\x80\x9d Hyacinth could\r\nsee that Inglefield was a subject she wished to turn off, and to do so\r\nshe added: \xe2\x80\x9cWe too are of all ages and sizes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it\xe2\x80\x99s fortunate you\xe2\x80\x99re not all _your_ size!\xe2\x80\x9d Paul Muniment\r\ndeclared with a freedom at which Hyacinth was rather shocked and which\r\nled him to suspect that though his new friend was a very fine fellow\r\na delicate tact was not his main characteristic. Later he explained\r\nthis by the fact that he was rural and provincial and had not had,\r\nlike himself, the benefit of the life of a capital; and later still he\r\nwished to know what, after all, such a character as that had to do with\r\ntact or with compliments, and why its work in the world was not most\r\nproperly performed by the simple exercise of a rude manly strength.\r\n\r\nAt this familiar allusion to her stature Lady Aurora turned hither and\r\nthither a little confusedly; Hyacinth saw her high, lean figure almost\r\nrock in the dim little room. Her commotion carried her to the door, and\r\nwith ejaculations of which it was difficult to guess the meaning she\r\nwas about to depart when Rosy detained her, having evidently much more\r\nsocial art than Paul. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you see it\xe2\x80\x99s only because her ladyship\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nstanding up that she\xe2\x80\x99s so, you gawk? _We\xe2\x80\x99re_ not thirteen, at any rate,\r\nand we\xe2\x80\x99ve got all the furniture we want, so there\xe2\x80\x99s a chair for every\r\none. Do be seated again, Lady Aurora, and help me to entertain this\r\ngentleman. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know your name, sir; perhaps my brother will mention\r\nit when he has collected his wits. I\xe2\x80\x99m very glad to see you, though I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t see you very well. Why shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t we light one of her ladyship\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncandles? It\xe2\x80\x99s very different to that common thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHyacinth thought Miss Muniment very charming; he had begun to make her\r\nout better by this time, and he watched her small wan, pointed face,\r\nframed on the pillow by thick black hair. She was a diminutive dark\r\nperson, pale and wasted with a lifelong infirmity; Hyacinth thought her\r\nmanner denoted high accomplishment\xe2\x80\x94he judged it impossible to tell her\r\nage. Lady Aurora pleaded that she ought to have gone, long since; but\r\nshe seated herself nevertheless on the chair that Paul pushed toward\r\nher.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere\xe2\x80\x99s a go!\xe2\x80\x9d this young man exclaimed to the other guest. \xe2\x80\x9cYou told\r\nme your name, but I\xe2\x80\x99ve clean forgotten it.\xe2\x80\x9d Then when Hyacinth had\r\npronounced it again he said to his sister: \xe2\x80\x9cThat won\xe2\x80\x99t tell you much;\r\nthere are bushels of Robinsons in the north. But you\xe2\x80\x99ll like him,\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99s all right; I met him at the Poupins.\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cPuppin\xe2\x80\x9d would represent\r\nthe sound by which he designated the French bookbinder, and that was\r\nthe name by which Hyacinth always heard him called at Crookenden\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\nHyacinth knew how much nearer to the right thing he himself came.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour name, like mine, represents a flower,\xe2\x80\x9d said the little woman in\r\nthe bed. \xe2\x80\x9cMine is Rose Muniment and her ladyship\xe2\x80\x99s is Aurora Langrish.\r\nThat means the morning or the dawn; it\xe2\x80\x99s the most beautiful of all,\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t you think?\xe2\x80\x9d Rose Muniment addressed this question to Hyacinth\r\nwhile Lady Aurora gazed at her shyly and mutely and as if admiring\r\nher manner, her self-possession and flow of conversation. Her brother\r\nlighted one of the visitor\xe2\x80\x99s candles and the girl went on without\r\nwaiting for Hyacinth\xe2\x80\x99s response. \xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t it right she should be called\r\nthe dawn when she brings light where she goes? The Puppins are the\r\ncharming foreigners I\xe2\x80\x99ve told you about,\xe2\x80\x9d she explained to her friend.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it\xe2\x80\x99s so pleasant knowing a few foreigners!\xe2\x80\x9d Lady Aurora exclaimed\r\nwith a spasm of expression. \xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re often so very fresh.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Robinson\xe2\x80\x99s a sort of foreigner and he\xe2\x80\x99s very fresh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Paul\r\nMuniment. \xe2\x80\x9cHe meets Mr. Puppin quite on his own ground. If I had his\r\ngift of tongues it would bring me on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sure I should be very happy to help you with your French. I\r\nfeel the advantage of knowing it,\xe2\x80\x9d Hyacinth remarked finely, becoming\r\nconscious that his declaration drew the attention of Lady Aurora toward\r\nhim; so that he wondered what he could go on to say to keep at that\r\nlevel. This was the first time he had encountered socially a member of\r\nthat aristocracy to which he had now for a good while known it was Miss\r\nPynsent\xe2\x80\x99s theory that he belonged; and the occasion was interesting in\r\nspite of the lady\xe2\x80\x99s appearing to have so few of the qualities of her\r\ncaste. She was about thirty years of age; her nose was large and, in\r\nspite of the sudden retreat of her chin, her face long and lean. She\r\nhad the manner of extreme near-sightedness; her front teeth projected\r\nfrom her upper gums, which she revealed when she smiled, and her fair\r\nhair, in tangled silky skeins (Rose Muniment thought it too lovely),\r\ndrooped over her pink cheeks. Her clothes looked as if she had worn\r\nthem a good deal in the rain, and the note of a certain disrepair in\r\nher apparel was given by a hole in one of her black gloves, through\r\nwhich a white finger gleamed. She was plain and diffident and she might\r\nhave been poor; but in the fine grain and sloping, shrinking slimness\r\nof her whole person, the delicacy of her curious features and a kind\r\nof cultivated quality in her sweet, vague, civil expression, there was\r\na suggestion of race, of long transmission, of an organism that had\r\nresulted from fortunate touch after touch. She was not a common woman;\r\nshe was one of the caprices of an aristocracy. Hyacinth didn\xe2\x80\x99t define\r\nher in this manner to himself, but he received from her the impression\r\nthat if she was a simple creature (which he learned later she was\r\nnot) aristocracies were yet complicated things. Lady Aurora remarked\r\nthat there were many delightful books in French, and he proclaimed\r\nit a torment to know that (as he did very well) when you saw no way\r\nto getting hold of them. This led Lady Aurora to say after a moment\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhesitation that she had a good lot of her own and that if he liked she\r\nshould be most happy to lend them to him. Hyacinth thanked her\xe2\x80\x94thanked\r\nher even too much, and felt both the kindness and the brilliant promise\r\nof the offer (he knew the exasperation of having volumes in his hands,\r\nfor external treatment, which he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t take home at night, having\r\ntried that method surreptitiously during his first weeks at old Crook\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nand come very near being sacked in consequence) while he wondered how\r\nsuch a system could be put into practice; whether she would expect him\r\nto call at her house and wait in the hall till the books were sent\r\nout to him. Rose Muniment exclaimed that that was her ladyship all\r\nover\xe2\x80\x94always wanting to make up to people for being less lucky than\r\nherself: she would take the shoes off her feet for any one that might\r\ntake a fancy to them. At this the visitor declared that she would stop\r\ncoming to see her if the girl caught her up that way for everything;\r\nand Rosy, without heeding the remonstrance, explained to Hyacinth that\r\nshe thought it the least she could do to give what she had. She was so\r\nashamed of being rich that she wondered the lower classes didn\xe2\x80\x99t break\r\ninto Inglefield and take possession of all the treasures in the Italian\r\nroom. She was a tremendous socialist; she was worse than any one\xe2\x80\x94she\r\nwas worse even than Paul.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder if she\xe2\x80\x99s worse than me,\xe2\x80\x9d Hyacinth returned at a venture,\r\nnot understanding the allusions to Inglefield and the Italian room,\r\nwhich Miss Muniment made as if she knew all about these places. After\r\nlearning more of the world he remembered this tone of Muniment\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsister\xe2\x80\x94he was to have plenty of observation of it on other occasions\xe2\x80\x94as\r\nthat of a person in the habit of visiting the nobility at their\r\ncountry-seats; she talked about Inglefield as if she had stayed there.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHullo, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know you were so advanced!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the master of\r\nthe scene, who had been sitting silent and sidewise in a chair that\r\nwas too narrow for him, his big arm hugging the back. \xe2\x80\x9cHave we been\r\nentertaining an angel unawares?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHyacinth made out he was chaffing him, but he knew the way to face that\r\nsort of thing was to exaggerate one\xe2\x80\x99s meaning. \xe2\x80\x9cYou didn\xe2\x80\x99t know I was\r\nadvanced? Why, I thought that was the principal thing about me. I think\r\nI go about as far as any one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought the principal thing about you was that you knew French,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nPaul Muniment said with an air of derision which showed him he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nput that ridicule upon him unless he liked him, at the same time that\r\nit revealed to him how he had come within an ace of posturing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know it for nothing. I\xe2\x80\x99ll say something that will take\r\nyour head off if you don\xe2\x80\x99t look out\xe2\x80\x94just the sort of thing they say so\r\nwell in French.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, do say something of that kind; we should enjoy it so much!\xe2\x80\x9d cried\r\nRosy in perfect good faith and clasping her hands for expectation.\r\n\r\nThe appeal was embarrassing, but Hyacinth was saved from the\r\nconsequences of it by a remark from Lady Aurora, who quavered out the\r\nwords after two or three false starts, appearing to address him, now\r\nthat she spoke to him directly, with a sort of overdone consideration.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should like so very much to know\xe2\x80\x94it would be so interesting\xe2\x80\x94if you\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t mind\xe2\x80\x94how far exactly you do go.\xe2\x80\x9d She threw back her head very far\r\nand thrust her shoulders forward, and if her chin had been more adapted\r\nto such a purpose would have appeared to point it at him.\r\n\r\nThis challenge was hardly less alarming than the other, for he was far\r\nfrom being ready with an impressive formula. He replied, however, with\r\na candour in which he tried as far as possible to sink his vagueness:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99m very strong indeed. He has a sweet assortment of phrases\xe2\x80\x94they\xe2\x80\x99re really pretty things\r\nto hear, some of them; but he hasn\xe2\x80\x99t had a new idea these thirty years.\r\nIt\xe2\x80\x99s the old stock that has been withering in the window. All the\r\nsame he warms one up; he has a spark of the sacred fire. The principal\r\nconclusion Mr. Robinson sees his way to,\xe2\x80\x9d he added to Lady Aurora, \xe2\x80\x9cis\r\nthat your father ought to have his head chopped off and carried on a\r\npike.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh yes, the French Revolution.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLord, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know anything about your father, my lady!\xe2\x80\x9d Hyacinth\r\ninterposed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDidn\xe2\x80\x99t you ever hear of the Earl of Inglefield?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Rose Muniment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s one of the best,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lady Aurora as if she were pleading for\r\nhim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery likely, but he\xe2\x80\x99s a landlord, and he has an hereditary seat and\r\na park of five thousand acres all to himself, while we\xe2\x80\x99re bundled\r\ntogether into this sort of kennel.\xe2\x80\x9d Hyacinth admired the young man\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nconsistency till he saw he was amusing himself; after which he still\r\nadmired the way he could mix that up with the tremendous opinions\r\nit must have been certain he entertained. In his own imagination he\r\nassociated bitterness with the revolutionary passion; but the young\r\nchemical expert, at the same time that he was planning far ahead,\r\nseemed capable of turning revolutionists themselves into ridicule even\r\nfor the entertainment of the revolutionised.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99ve told you often enough that I don\xe2\x80\x99t go with you at all,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Rose Muniment, whose recumbency appeared not in the least to\r\ninterfere with her universal participation. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll make a tremendous\r\nmistake if you try to turn everything round. There ought to be\r\ndifferences, and high and low, and there always will be, true as ever\r\nI lie here. I think it\xe2\x80\x99s against everything, pulling down them that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nabove.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEverything points to great changes in this country, but if once our\r\nRosy\xe2\x80\x99s against them how can you be sure? That\xe2\x80\x99s the only thing that\r\nmakes me doubt,\xe2\x80\x9d her brother went on, looking at her with a placidity\r\nwhich showed the habit of indulgence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I may be ill, but I ain\xe2\x80\x99t buried, and if I\xe2\x80\x99m content with my\r\nposition\xe2\x80\x94such a position as it is\xe2\x80\x94surely other folk might be with\r\ntheirs. Her ladyship may think I\xe2\x80\x99m as good as her if she takes that\r\nnotion; but she\xe2\x80\x99ll have a deal to do to make me believe it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you\xe2\x80\x99re much better than I, and I know very few people so good\r\nas you,\xe2\x80\x9d Lady Aurora brought out, blushing not for her opinions but for\r\nher timidity. It was easy to see that though she was original she would\r\nhave liked to be even more original than she was. She was conscious,\r\nhowever, that such a declaration might appear rather gross to persons\r\nwho didn\xe2\x80\x99t see exactly how she meant it; so she added, as quickly as\r\nher hesitating manner permitted, to cover it up: \xe2\x80\x9cYou know there\xe2\x80\x99s one\r\nthing you ought to remember, _\xc3\xa0 propos_ of revolutions and changes and\r\nall that sort of thing; I just mention it because we were talking of\r\nsome of the dreadful things that were done in France. If there were to\r\nbe a great disturbance in this country\xe2\x80\x94and of course one hopes there\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94it would be my impression that the people would behave in a\r\ndifferent way altogether.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat people do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d Hyacinth allowed himself to inquire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, the upper class, the people who\xe2\x80\x99ve got all the things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe don\xe2\x80\x99t call them the _people_,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Hyacinth, reflecting the\r\nnext instant that his remark was a little primitive.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose you call them the wretches, the scoundrels!\xe2\x80\x9d Rose Muniment\r\nsuggested, laughing merrily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll the things, but not all the brains,\xe2\x80\x9d her brother said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo indeed, aren\xe2\x80\x99t they stupid?\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed her ladyship. \xe2\x80\x9cAll the same,\r\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t think they\xe2\x80\x99d all go abroad.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo abroad?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mean like the French nobles who emigrated so much. They\xe2\x80\x99d stay at\r\nhome and resist; they\xe2\x80\x99d make more of a fight. When her ladyship says the aristocracy will make a fine stand I\r\nlike to hear her say it and I think she speaks in a manner that becomes\r\nher own position. But there\xe2\x80\x99s something else in her tone which, if\r\nI may be allowed to say so, I think a great mistake. If her ladyship\r\nexpects, in case of the lower classes coming up in that odious manner,\r\nto be let off easily, for the sake of the concessions she may have made\r\nin advance, I\xe2\x80\x99d just advise her to save herself the disappointment\r\nand the trouble. They won\xe2\x80\x99t be a bit the wiser and they won\xe2\x80\x99t either\r\nknow or care. They\xe2\x80\x99ll trample on her just the same as on the\r\nothers, and they\xe2\x80\x99ll say she has got to pay for her title and her grand\r\nrelations and her fine appearance. Therefore I advise her not to waste\r\nher good-nature in trying to let herself down. When you\xe2\x80\x99re up so high\r\nas that you\xe2\x80\x99ve got to stay there; and if the powers above have made you\r\na lady the best thing you can do is to hold up your head. I can promise\r\nyour ladyship _I_ would!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe close logic of this speech and the quaint self-possession with\r\nwhich the little bedridden speaker delivered it struck Hyacinth as\r\namazing and confirmed his idea that the brother and sister were a\r\nmost extraordinary pair. It had a terrible effect on poor Lady Aurora,\r\nby whom so stern a lesson from so humble a quarter had evidently not\r\nbeen expected and who sought refuge from her confusion in a series of\r\npleading gasps, while Paul Muniment, with his humorous density, which\r\nwas deliberate, and acute too, not seeing, or at any rate not heeding,\r\nthat she had been sufficiently snubbed by his sister, inflicted a fresh\r\nhumiliation in saying: \xe2\x80\x9cRosy\xe2\x80\x99s right, my lady. It\xe2\x80\x99s no use trying to\r\nbuy yourself off. You can\xe2\x80\x99t do enough; your sacrifices don\xe2\x80\x99t count.\r\nYou spoil your fun now and you don\xe2\x80\x99t get it made up to you later. To\r\nall you people nothing will ever be made up. Eat your pudding while you\r\nhave it; you mayn\xe2\x80\x99t have it long.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLady Aurora listened to him with her eyes on his face, and as they\r\nrested there Hyacinth scarcely knew what to make of her expression.\r\nAfterwards he thought he could attach a meaning to it. She got up\r\nquickly when Muniment had ceased speaking; the movement suggested she\r\nhad taken offence and he would have liked to show her he thought she\r\nhad been rather roughly used. But she gave him no chance, not glancing\r\nat him for a moment. Then he saw he was mistaken and that if she had\r\nflushed considerably it was only with the excitement of pleasure, the\r\nenjoyment of such original talk and of seeing her friends at last as\r\nfree and familiar as she wished them to be. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re the most delightful\r\npeople\xe2\x80\x94I wish every one could know you!\xe2\x80\x9d she broke out. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I must\r\nreally be going.\xe2\x80\x9d She went to the bed and bent over Rosy and kissed\r\nher.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPaul will see you as far as you like on your way home,\xe2\x80\x9d this young\r\nwoman remarked.\r\n\r\nLady Aurora protested, but Paul, without protesting in return, only\r\ntook up his hat and smiled at her as if he knew his duty. On this her\r\nladyship said: \xe2\x80\x9cWell, you may see me downstairs; I forgot it was so\r\ndark.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must take her ladyship\xe2\x80\x99s own candle and you must call a cab,\xe2\x80\x9d Rosy\r\ndirected.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I don\xe2\x80\x99t go in cabs. I walk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you may go on the top of a \xe2\x80\x99bus if you like; you can\xe2\x80\x99t help\r\nbeing superb,\xe2\x80\x9d Miss Muniment declared, watching her sympathetically.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuperb? Oh mercy!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the poor devoted, grotesque lady, leaving\r\nthe room with Paul, who told Hyacinth to wait for him a little. She\r\nneglected to take leave of our young man, and he asked himself what was\r\nto be hoped from that sort of people when even the best of them\xe2\x80\x94those\r\nthat wished to be agreeable to the _demos_\xe2\x80\x94reverted inevitably to the\r\nsupercilious. She had said no more about lending him her books.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIX\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe lives in Belgrave Square; she has ever so many brothers and\r\nsisters; one of her sisters is married to Lord Warmington,\xe2\x80\x9d Rose\r\nMuniment instantly began, not apparently in the least discomposed at\r\nbeing left alone with a strange young man in a room which was now half\r\ndark again, thanks to her brother\xe2\x80\x99s having carried off the second and\r\nmore brilliant candle. She was so interested for the time in telling\r\nHyacinth the history of Lady Aurora that she appeared not to remember\r\nhow little she knew about himself. Her ladyship had dedicated her\r\nlife and her pocket-money to the poor and sick; she cared nothing for\r\nparties and races and dances and picnics and cards and life in great\r\nhouses, the usual amusements of the aristocracy: she was like one of\r\nthe saints of old come to life again out of a legend. She had made\r\ntheir acquaintance, Paul\xe2\x80\x99s and hers, about a year before, through\r\na friend of theirs, such a fine brave young woman, who was in Saint\r\nThomas\xe2\x80\x99s Hospital for a surgical operation. She had been laid up there\r\nfor weeks during which Lady Aurora, always looking out for those who\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t help themselves, used to come and talk to her and read to her,\r\ntill the end of her time in the ward, when the poor girl, parting with\r\nher kind friend, told her how she knew of another unfortunate creature\r\n(for whom there was no place there, because she was incurable) who\r\nwould be mighty thankful for any little attention of that sort. She\r\nhad given Lady Aurora the address in Audley Court and the very next\r\nday her ladyship had knocked at their door. It wasn\xe2\x80\x99t because she was\r\npoor\xe2\x80\x94though in all conscience they were pinched enough\xe2\x80\x94but because\r\nshe had so little satisfaction in her limbs. Lady Aurora came very\r\noften, for several months, without meeting Paul, because he was always\r\nat his work; but one day he came home early on purpose to find her,\r\nto thank her for her goodness, and also to see (Miss Muniment rather\r\nshyly intimated) if she were really so good as his extravagant little\r\nsister made her out. Rosy had a triumph after that: Paul had to admit\r\nthat her ladyship was beyond anything that any one in his waking senses\r\nwould believe. She seemed to want to give up everything to those who\r\nwere below her and never to expect any thanks at all. And she wasn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nalways preaching and showing you your duty; she wanted to talk to\r\nyou sociable-like, as if you were just her own sister. And _her_ own\r\nsisters were the highest in the land, and you might see her name in\r\nthe newspapers the day they were presented to the Queen. Lady Aurora\r\nhad been presented too, with feathers in her head and a long tail to\r\nher gown; but she had turned her back on it all with a kind of terror\xe2\x80\x94a\r\nsort of shivering sinking state which she had often described to Miss\r\nMuniment. The day she had first seen Paul was the day they became so\r\nintimate, the three of them together\xe2\x80\x94if she might apply such a word as\r\nthat to such a peculiar connexion. The little woman, the little girl,\r\nas she lay there (Hyacinth scarce knew how to characterise her) told\r\nour young man a very great secret, in which he found himself too much\r\ninterested to think of criticising so precipitate a confidence. The\r\nsecret was that, of all the people she had ever seen in the world, her\r\nladyship thought Rosy\xe2\x80\x99s Paul the very cleverest. And she had seen the\r\ngreatest, the most famous, the brightest of every kind, for they all\r\ncame to stay at Inglefield, thirty and forty of them at once. She had\r\ntalked with them all and heard them say their best (and you could fancy\r\nhow they would try to give it out at such a place as that, where there\r\nwas nearly a mile of conservatories and a hundred wax candles were\r\nlighted at a time) and at the end of it all she had made the remark to\r\nherself\xe2\x80\x94and she had made it to Rosy too\xe2\x80\x94that there was none of them\r\nhad such a head on his shoulders as the young man in Audley Court.\r\nRosy wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t spread such a rumour as that in the court itself, but\r\nshe wanted every friend of her brother\xe2\x80\x99s (and she could see Hyacinth\r\nwas a real one by the way he listened) to know what was thought of\r\nhim by them that had an experience of intellect. She didn\xe2\x80\x99t wish to\r\ngive it out that her ladyship had lowered herself in any manner to\r\na person that earned his bread in a dirty shop (clever as he _might_\r\nbe), but it was easy to see she minded what he said as if he had been\r\na bishop\xe2\x80\x94or more indeed, for she didn\xe2\x80\x99t think much of bishops, any\r\nmore than Paul himself, and that was an idea she had got from him.\r\nOh, she took it none so ill if he came back from his work before\r\nshe had gone, and to-night Hyacinth could see for himself how she\r\nhad lingered. This evening, she was sure, her ladyship would let him\r\nwalk home with her half the way. This announcement gave Hyacinth the\r\nprospect of a considerable session with his communicative hostess; but\r\nhe was very glad to wait, for he was vaguely, strangely excited by her\r\ntalk, fascinated by the little queer-smelling, high-perched interior,\r\nencumbered with relics, treasured and polished, of a poor north-country\r\nhome, bedecked with penny ornaments and related in so unexpected a\r\nmanner to Belgrave Square and the great landed estates. He spent half\r\nan hour with Paul Muniment\xe2\x80\x99s small, odd, sharp, crippled, chattering\r\nsister, who gave him an impression of education and native wit (she\r\nexpressed herself far better than Pinnie or than Milly Henning) and\r\nwho startled, puzzled and at the same time rather distressed him by\r\nthe manner in which she referred herself to the most abject class\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nclass that prostrated itself, that was in a fever and flutter, in\r\nthe presence of its betters. That was Pinnie\xe2\x80\x99s attitude of course,\r\nbut Hyacinth had long ago perceived that his adoptive mother had\r\ngenerations of plebeian patience in her blood, and that though she had\r\na tender soul she had not a truly high spirit. He was more entertained\r\nthan afflicted, however, by Miss Muniment\xe2\x80\x99s tone, and he was thrilled\r\nby the frequency and familiarity of her allusions to a kind of life\r\nhe had often wondered about; this was the first time he had heard it\r\ndescribed with that degree of authority. By the nature of his mind he\r\nwas perpetually, almost morbidly conscious that the circle in which he\r\nlived was an infinitesimally small shallow eddy in the roaring vortex\r\nof London, and his imagination plunged again and again into the flood\r\nthat whirled past it and round it, in the hope of being carried to some\r\nbrighter, happier vision\xe2\x80\x94the vision of societies where, in splendid\r\nrooms, with smiles and soft voices, distinguished men, with women who\r\nwere both proud and gentle, talked of art, literature and history.\r\nWhen Rosy had delivered herself to her complete satisfaction on the\r\nsubject of Lady Aurora she became more quiet, asking as yet, however,\r\nno straight questions of her guest, whom she seemed to take very much\r\nfor granted. He presently remarked that she must let him come very soon\r\nagain, and he added, to explain this wish: \xe2\x80\x9cYou know you seem to me\r\nvery curious people.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Muniment didn\xe2\x80\x99t in the least repudiate the imputation. \xe2\x80\x9cOh yes,\r\nI daresay we seem very curious. I think we\xe2\x80\x99re generally thought so;\r\nespecially me, being so miserable and yet so lively.\xe2\x80\x9d And she laughed\r\ntill her bed creaked again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps it\xe2\x80\x99s lucky you\xe2\x80\x99re ill; perhaps if you had your health you\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nbe all over the place,\xe2\x80\x9d Hyacinth suggested. And he went on candidly: \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t make it out, your being so up in everything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see why you need make it out! But you would, perhaps, if you\r\nhad known my father and mother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWere they such a rare lot?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you\xe2\x80\x99d say so if you had ever been in the mines. Yes, in the\r\nmines, where the filthy coal\xe2\x80\x99s dug out. That\xe2\x80\x99s where my father came\r\nfrom\xe2\x80\x94he was working in the pit when he was a child of ten. He never\r\nhad a day\xe2\x80\x99s schooling in his life, but he climbed up out of his black\r\nhole into daylight and air, and he invented a machine, and he married\r\nmy mother, who came out of Durham, and (by her people) out of the pits\r\nand the awfulness too. My father had no great figure, but _she_ was\r\nmagnificent\xe2\x80\x94the finest woman in the country and the bravest and the\r\nbest. She\xe2\x80\x99s in her grave now, and I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t go to look at it even if\r\nit were in the nearest churchyard. My father was as black as the coal\r\nhe worked in: I know I\xe2\x80\x99m just his pattern, barring that _he_ did have\r\nhis legs, when the liquor hadn\xe2\x80\x99t got into them. Yet between him and my\r\nmother, for grand high intelligence, there wasn\xe2\x80\x99t much to choose. But\r\nwhat\xe2\x80\x99s the use of brains if you haven\xe2\x80\x99t got a backbone? My poor father\r\nhad even less of that than I, for with me it\xe2\x80\x99s only the body that can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nstand up, and with him it was the very nature. He invented, for use\r\nin machine-shops, a mechanical improvement\xe2\x80\x94a new kind of beam-fixing,\r\nwhatever that is\xe2\x80\x94and he sold it at Bradford for fifteen pounds: I mean\r\nthe whole right and profit of it and every hope and comfort of his\r\nfamily. He was always straying and my mother was always bringing him\r\nback. She had plenty to do, with me a puny ailing brat from the moment\r\nI opened my eyes. Well, one night he strayed so far that he never came\r\nhome, or only came a loose bloody bundle of clothes. He had fallen into\r\na gravel-pit, he didn\xe2\x80\x99t know where he was going. That\xe2\x80\x99s the reason my\r\nbrother won\xe2\x80\x99t ever touch so much as you could wet your finger with, and\r\nthat I\xe2\x80\x99ve only a drop once a week or so in the way of a strengthener. I\r\ntake what her ladyship brings me, but I take no more. If she could but\r\nhave come to us before my mother went\xe2\x80\x94that would have been a saving! I\r\nwas only nine when my father died, and I\xe2\x80\x99m three years older than Paul.\r\nMy mother did for us with all her might, and she kept us decent\xe2\x80\x94if\r\nsuch a useless little mess as me can be said to be decent. At any rate\r\nshe kept me alive, and that\xe2\x80\x99s a proof she was handy. She went to the\r\nwash-tub, and she might have been a queen as she stood there with her\r\nbare arms in the foul linen and her long hair braided on her head. She\r\nwas terrible handsome, but he\xe2\x80\x99d have been a bold man that had taken on\r\nhimself to tell her so. And it was from her we got our education\xe2\x80\x94she\r\nwas determined we should rise above the common. You might have thought,\r\nin her position, that she couldn\xe2\x80\x99t go into such things, but she was a\r\nrare one for keeping you at your book. She could hold to her idea when\r\nmy poor father couldn\xe2\x80\x99t, and her idea for us was that Paul should get\r\nlearning and should look after me. You can see for yourself that that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwhat has come of it. How he got it\xe2\x80\x99s more than I can say, as we never\r\nhad a penny to pay for it; and of course my mother\xe2\x80\x99s head wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have\r\nbeen of much use if he hadn\xe2\x80\x99t had a head himself. Well, it was all in\r\nthe family. Paul was a boy that would learn more from a yellow poster\r\non a wall or a time-table at a railway station than many a young fellow\r\nfrom a year at college. That was his only college, poor lad\xe2\x80\x94picking\r\nup what he could. Mother was taken when she was still needed, nearly\r\nfive years ago. There was an epidemic of typhoid, and of course it\r\nmust pass me over, the goose of a thing\xe2\x80\x94only that I\xe2\x80\x99d have made a poor\r\nfeast\xe2\x80\x94and just lay that really grand character on her back. Well, she\r\nnever again made it ache over her soapsuds, straight and broad as it\r\nwas. Not having seen her, you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t believe,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rose Muniment in\r\nconclusion; \xe2\x80\x9cbut I just wanted you to understand that our parents had\r\njolly good brains at least to give us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHyacinth listened to this eloquence\xe2\x80\x94the clearest statement of anything\r\nhe had ever heard made by a woman\xe2\x80\x94with the deepest interest, and\r\nwithout being in the least moved to allow for filial exaggeration;\r\ninasmuch as his impression of the brother and sister was such as it\r\nwould have taken a much more marvellous tale to account for. The very\r\nway Rose Muniment talked of brains made him feel this; she pronounced\r\nthe word as if she were distributing prizes for intellectual eminence\r\nfrom off a platform. No doubt the weak inventor and the strong worker\r\nhad been fine specimens, but that didn\xe2\x80\x99t diminish the merit of their\r\nhighly original offspring. The girl\xe2\x80\x99s insistence on her mother\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nvirtues (even now that her age had become more definite to him he\r\nthought of her as a girl) touched in his heart a chord that was\r\nalways ready to throb\xe2\x80\x94the chord of melancholy aimless wonder as to the\r\ndifference it would have made for his life to have had some rich warm\r\npresence like that in it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you very fond of your brother?\xe2\x80\x9d he inquired after a little.\r\n\r\nThe eyes of his hostess glittered at him. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you ever quarrel with him\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ll see whose side I shall take.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, before that I shall make you like _me_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s very possible, and you\xe2\x80\x99ll see how I shall fling you over!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy then do you object so to his views\xe2\x80\x94his ideas about the way the\r\npeople are to come up?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause I think he\xe2\x80\x99ll get over them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever\xe2\x80\x94never!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Hyacinth. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve only known him an hour or two, but\r\nI deny that with all my strength.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that the way you\xe2\x80\x99re going to make me like you\xe2\x80\x94contradicting me so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nMiss Muniment asked with familiar archness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the use, when you tell me I shall be sacrificed? One might as\r\nwell perish for a lamb as for a sheep.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t believe you\xe2\x80\x99re a lamb at all. But Rosy\r\nwent on before he had time to do more than reflect that there would\r\nevidently be great things to learn about her brother: \xe2\x80\x9cI haven\xe2\x80\x99t the\r\nleast objection to seeing the people improved, but I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to\r\nsee the aristocracy lowered an inch. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what you take us for! I never saw him lose his\r\ntemper in his life.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe must be a rum customer! I think she\r\nmust be rather crowded out at home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCrowded out of Inglefield? He attributed a remarkable meaning to\r\nit; it seemed to say that people such as he were now so sure of their\r\ngame that they could afford to be magnanimous; or else it expressed\r\na prevision of the doom that hung over her ladyship\xe2\x80\x99s head. Muniment\r\nasked if Hyacinth and Rosy had got on together, and the girl replied\r\nthat Mr. Robinson had made himself most agreeable. \xe2\x80\x9cThen you must tell\r\nme all about him after he goes, for you know I don\xe2\x80\x99t know him much\r\nmyself,\xe2\x80\x9d said her brother.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes, I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you everything\xe2\x80\x94you know how I like describing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHyacinth found himself amused at the young lady\xe2\x80\x99s account of his\r\nefforts to please her, the fact being that he had only listened to\r\nher own eager discourse without opening his mouth; but Paul, whether\r\nor no guessing the truth, said to him all pertinently: \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s very\r\nwonderful\xe2\x80\x94she can describe things she has never seen. And they\xe2\x80\x99re just\r\nlike the reality.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s nothing I\xe2\x80\x99ve never seen,\xe2\x80\x9d Rosy declared. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the advantage\r\nof my lying here in such a manner. Is that the party you want to belong to?\xe2\x80\x9d he went on with his\r\nclear eyes ranging over his diminutive friend.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you\xe2\x80\x99ll show me the thing itself I shall have no more occasion to\r\nmind the newspapers,\xe2\x80\x9d Hyacinth candidly pleaded, rejoicing all the\r\nwhile to feel himself in such a relation. It was his view of himself,\r\nand not an unfair one, that his was a character that would never sue\r\nfor a favour; but now he felt that in any connexion with Paul Muniment\r\nsuch a law would be suspended. This rare man he could go on his knees\r\nto without a sense of humiliation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat thing do you mean, infatuated, deluded youth?\xe2\x80\x9d Paul pursued,\r\nrefusing to be serious.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you know you do go to places you had far better keep out of, and\r\nthat often when I lie here and listen to steps on the stairs I\xe2\x80\x99m sure\r\nthey are coming in to make a search for your papers,\xe2\x80\x9d Miss Muniment\r\nlucidly interposed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe day they find my papers, my dear, will be the day you\xe2\x80\x99ll get up\r\nand dance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did you ask me to come home with you for?\xe2\x80\x9d Hyacinth demanded\r\nas he twirled his hat. It was an effort for a moment to keep the\r\ntears from his eyes; he saw himself forced to put such a different\r\nconstruction on his new friend\xe2\x80\x99s hospitality. He had had a happy\r\nimpression that Muniment had divined in him a possible associate of\r\na high type in a subterranean crusade against the existing order of\r\nthings, whereas it now came over him that the real use he had been put\r\nto was to beguile an hour for a pert invalid. This young man justified at the\r\npresent juncture the high estimate Lady Aurora Langrish had formed of\r\nhis intelligence: whatever his natural reply to Hyacinth\xe2\x80\x99s question\r\nwould have been he invented straight off a better one and said at\r\nrandom, smiling and not knowing exactly what his visitor had meant:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did I ask you to come with me for? Just meet me some night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere, where?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Hyacinth eagerly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you where when we get away from _her_.\xe2\x80\x9d And Muniment led\r\nhim good-humouredly out.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nX\r\n\r\n\r\nSeveral months after Hyacinth had made his acquaintance, Millicent\r\nHenning remarked that it was high time our hero should take her to some\r\nfirst-class place of amusement. He proposed hereupon the Canterbury\r\nMusic Hall; at which she tossed her head and affirmed that when a young\r\nlady had done for a young man what she had done for him the least he\r\ncould do was to give her an evening at some theatre in the Strand.\r\nHyacinth would have been a good deal at a loss to say exactly what she\r\nhad done for him, but it was familiar to him by this time that she\r\nregarded him as under great obligations. From the day she had come\r\nto look him up in Lomax Place she had taken a position, largely, in\r\nhis life, and he had seen poor Pinnie\xe2\x80\x99s wan countenance grow several\r\ndegrees more blank. Amanda Pynsent\xe2\x80\x99s forebodings had been answered to\r\nthe letter; the flaring cometary creature had become a fixed star. She\r\nhad never spoken to him of Millicent but once, several weeks after her\r\ninterview with the girl; and this had not been in a tone of rebuke, for\r\nshe had divested herself for ever of any maternal prerogative. Tearful,\r\ntremulous, deferential inquiry was now her only weapon, and nothing\r\ncould be more humble and circumspect than the manner in which she made\r\nuse of it. He was never at home of an evening, at present, and he had\r\nmysterious ways of spending his Sundays, with which church-going had\r\nnothing to do. The time had been when often, after tea, he sat near\r\nthe lamp with the dressmaker and, while her fingers flew, read out to\r\nher the works of Dickens and of Scott; happy hours of vain semblance\r\nthat he had forgotten the wrong she had done him, so that she could\r\nalmost forget it herself. But now he gulped down his tea so fast that\r\nhe hardly took off his hat while he sat there, and Pinnie, with her\r\nquick eye for all matters of costume, noticed that he wore it still\r\nmore gracefully askew than usual, cocking it with a victorious exalted\r\nair. He hummed to himself; he fingered his moustache; he looked out\r\nof window when there was nothing to look at; he seemed preoccupied,\r\nlaunched in intellectual excursions, half anxious and half in spirits.\r\nDuring the whole winter Miss Pynsent explained everything by four words\r\nmurmured beneath her breath: \xe2\x80\x9cThat beastly forward jade!\xe2\x80\x9d On the single\r\noccasion, however, on which she had sought relief from her agitation in\r\nan appeal to Hyacinth she didn\xe2\x80\x99t trust herself to designate the girl by\r\nepithet or title.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s only one thing I want to know,\xe2\x80\x9d she said to him in a manner\r\nwhich might have seemed casual if in her silence, knowing her as well\r\nas he did, he had not already perceived the implication of her thought.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes she expect you to marry her, dearest?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes who expect me? I should like to see the woman who does!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course you know who I mean. The one that came after you\xe2\x80\x94and picked\r\nyou right up\xe2\x80\x94from the other end of London.\xe2\x80\x9d And at the remembrance\r\nof that insufferable scene poor Pinnie flamed for a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cAren\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nthere plenty of vulgar fellows in that low part where she lives without\r\nher ravaging over here? Why can\xe2\x80\x99t she stick to her own beat, I should\r\nlike to know?\xe2\x80\x9d Hyacinth had flushed at the question, and she had seen\r\nsomething in his face to make her change her tone. \xe2\x80\x9cJust promise me\r\nthis, my precious child: that if you get into any sort of mess with\r\nthat piece you\xe2\x80\x99ll immediately confide it to your poor old Pinnie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy poor old Pinnie sometimes makes me quite sick,\xe2\x80\x9d he remarked for\r\nanswer. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat sort of a mess do you suppose I shall get into?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, suppose she does come it over you that you promised to marry\r\nher?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t know what you\xe2\x80\x99re talking about. She doesn\xe2\x80\x99t want to marry\r\nany one\xe2\x80\x94the way she sees it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen how the dickens does she see it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you imagine I\xe2\x80\x99d tell a lady\xe2\x80\x99s secrets?\xe2\x80\x9d the young man returned.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh laws, if she was a lady I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be afraid!\xe2\x80\x9d said Pinnie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEvery woman\xe2\x80\x99s a lady when she has placed herself under one\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nprotection,\xe2\x80\x9d Hyacinth declared with his little manner of a man of the\r\ngreat world.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnder your protection? Oh I say!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Pinnie, staring. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd pray\r\nwho\xe2\x80\x99s to protect you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs soon as she had said this she repented, because it seemed just the\r\nsort of exclamation that would have made Hyacinth bite her head off.\r\nOne of the things she loved him for, however, was that he gave you\r\ntouching surprises in this line, had sudden inconsistencies of temper\r\nthat were all for your advantage. He was by no means always mild when\r\nhe ought to have been, but he was sometimes heavenly when he needn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nhave been at all. At such moments Pinnie wanted to kiss him and had\r\noften tried to make Mr. Vetch understand what fascinating traits of\r\ncharacter she was always noting in their young friend. This particular\r\none was rather difficult to describe, and Mr. Vetch never would admit\r\nthat he understood, or that he had observed anything that seemed to\r\ncorrespond to the dressmaker\xe2\x80\x99s somewhat confused psychological sketch.\r\nIt was a comfort to her in these days, and almost the only one she had,\r\nthat she was sure Anastasius Vetch understood a good deal more than he\r\nfelt bound to acknowledge. He was always up to his old game of being\r\na great deal cleverer than cleverness itself required; and it consoled\r\nher present weak, pinched feeling to know that, though he still talked\r\nof the boy as if it would be a pity to take him too seriously, that\r\nwasn\xe2\x80\x99t the way he thought of him. Miss Pynsent went so far\r\nas to say to herself that the fiddler probably had savings and that no\r\none had ever known of any one else belonging to him. She wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have\r\nmentioned it to Hyacinth for the world, for fear of leading up to a\r\ndisappointment; but she had visions of a foolscap sheet folded away in\r\nsome queer little bachelor\xe2\x80\x99s box (she couldn\xe2\x80\x99t fancy what men kept in\r\nsuch places) on which the youth\xe2\x80\x99s name would have been written down in\r\nvery big letters before a solicitor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I\xe2\x80\x99m unprotected in the nature of things,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied, smiling at\r\nhis too scrupulous companion. She won\xe2\x80\x99t drag me to the altar.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd pray doesn\xe2\x80\x99t she think you good enough\xe2\x80\x94for one of the beautiful\r\n\xe2\x80\x99Ennings?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t understand, my poor Pinnie,\xe2\x80\x9d he wearily pleaded. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nsometimes think there isn\xe2\x80\x99t a single thing in life that you understand.\r\nOne of these days she\xe2\x80\x99ll marry an alderman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn alderman\xe2\x80\x94that creature?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn alderman or a banker or a bishop or some of that bloated kind. But she was always delighted when he made a\r\nremark that showed he was conscious of being of fine clay\xe2\x80\x94flashed out\r\nan allusion to his not being what he seemed. He was not what he seemed,\r\nbut even with Pinnie\xe2\x80\x99s valuable assistance he had not succeeded in\r\nrepresenting to himself very definitely what he was. She had placed\r\nat his disposal for this purpose a passionate idealism which, employed\r\nin some case where it could have consequences, might have been termed\r\nprofligate and which yet never cost her a scruple or a compunction.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sure a princess might look at you and be none the worse!\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\ndeclared in her delight at this assurance, more positive than any she\r\nhad yet received, that he was safe from the worse danger. Still it came over her that his taste might be\r\nlowered, and before the subject was dropped, on the present occasion,\r\nshe said that of course he must be quite aware of all that was wanting\r\nto such a girl as Millicent \xe2\x80\x99Enning\xe2\x80\x94who visibly wasn\xe2\x80\x99t worth any\r\nstruggle for her aspirate.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I don\xe2\x80\x99t bother about what\xe2\x80\x99s wanting to her.
