To rest my eyes on her beautiful face.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you come to England for that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor what else should I have come?\xe2\x80\x9d the Prince asked as he turned his\r\nblighted gaze to the opposite side of South Street.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn London, such a day as this, _gi\xc3\xa0_,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old lady\r\nsympathetically. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m very sorry for you; but if I had known you were\r\ncoming I\xe2\x80\x99d have written to you that you might spare yourself the pain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe gave a deep interminable sigh. \xe2\x80\x9cYou ask me what I wish to propose.\r\nWhat I wish to propose is that my wife shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t kill me inch by inch.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99d be much more likely to do that if you lived with her!\xe2\x80\x9d Madame\r\nGrandoni cried.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Cara amica_, she doesn\xe2\x80\x99t appear to have killed you,\xe2\x80\x9d the melancholy\r\nnobleman returned.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, me? I\xe2\x80\x99m past killing. I\xe2\x80\x99m as hard as a stone. I went through my\r\nmiseries long ago; I suffered what you\xe2\x80\x99ve not had to suffer; I wished\r\nfor death many times and I survived it all. Our troubles don\xe2\x80\x99t kill\r\nus, _Principe mio_; it\xe2\x80\x99s we who must try to kill them. I\xe2\x80\x99ve buried not\r\na few. Besides, Christina\xe2\x80\x99s fond of me, the devil knows why!\xe2\x80\x9d Madame\r\nGrandoni added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you\xe2\x80\x99re so good to her,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Prince, who laid his hand on her\r\nfat wrinkled fist.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Che vuole?_ I\xe2\x80\x99ve known her so long. And she has some such great\r\nqualities.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, to whom do you say it?\xe2\x80\x9d And he gazed at his boots again, for some\r\nmoments, in silence. Suddenly he resumed: \xe2\x80\x9cHow does she look to-day?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe always looks the same: like an angel who came down from heaven\r\nyesterday and has been rather disappointed in her first day on earth!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Prince was evidently a man of a simple nature, and Madame\r\nGrandoni\xe2\x80\x99s rather violent metaphor took his fancy. His face lighted up\r\na little and he replied with eagerness: \xe2\x80\x9cAh, she\xe2\x80\x99s the only woman I\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\never seen whose beauty never for a moment falls below itself. She has\r\nno bad days. She\xe2\x80\x99s so handsome when she\xe2\x80\x99s angry!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s very handsome to-day, but she\xe2\x80\x99s not angry,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old lady.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot when my name was announced?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was not with her then; but when she sent for me and asked me to see\r\nyou it was quite without passion. And even when I argued with her and\r\ntried to persuade her (and she doesn\xe2\x80\x99t like that, you know) she was\r\nstill perfectly quiet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe hates me, she despises me too much, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can I tell, dear Prince, when she never mentions you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever, never?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s much better than if she railed at you and abused you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean it should give me more hope for the future?\xe2\x80\x9d the young man\r\nasked quickly.\r\n\r\nHis old friend had a pause. \xe2\x80\x9cI mean it\xe2\x80\x99s better for _me_,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered\r\nwith a laugh of which the friendly ring covered as much as possible her\r\nequivocation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, you like me enough to care,\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured as he turned on her his\r\nsad grateful eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m very sorry for you. _Ma che vuole?_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Prince had apparently nothing to suggest and only exhaled in reply\r\nanother gloomy groan. Then he inquired if his wife pleased herself in\r\nthat country and if she intended to pass the summer in London. Would\r\nshe remain long in England and\xe2\x80\x94might he take the liberty to ask?\xe2\x80\x94what\r\nwere her plans? Madame Grandoni explained that the Princess had\r\nfound the English capital much more to her taste than one might have\r\nexpected, and that as for plans she had as many or as few as she had\r\nalways had. Had he ever known her to carry out any arrangement or to\r\ndo anything of any kind she had prepared or promised? She always at\r\nthe last moment did the other thing, the one that had been out of the\r\nquestion; and it was for this Madame Grandoni herself privately made\r\nher preparations. Christina, now that everything was over, would leave\r\nLondon from one day to the other; but they shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t know where they\r\nwere going till they arrived. The old lady concluded by asking if the\r\nPrince himself liked England. He thrust forward his full lips. \xe2\x80\x9cHow can\r\nI like anything? Besides, I\xe2\x80\x99ve been here before; I\xe2\x80\x99ve many friends.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis companion saw he had more to say to her, to extract from her,\r\nbut that he was hesitating nervously because he feared to incur some\r\nwarning, some rebuff with which his dignity\xe2\x80\x94in spite of his position\r\nof discomfiture, really very great\xe2\x80\x94might find it difficult to square\r\nitself. He looked vaguely round the room and presently remarked: \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nwanted to see for myself how she\xe2\x80\x99s living.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, that\xe2\x80\x99s very natural.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve heard\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ve heard\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d And Prince Casamassima stopped.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve heard great rubbish, I\xe2\x80\x99ve no doubt.\xe2\x80\x9d Madame Grandoni watched\r\nhim as if she foresaw what was coming.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe spends a terrible deal of money,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed she does.\xe2\x80\x9d The old lady knew that, careful as he was of\r\nhis very considerable property, which at one time had required much\r\nnursing, his wife\xe2\x80\x99s prodigality was not what lay heaviest on his mind.\r\nShe also knew that expensive and luxurious as Christina might be she\r\nhad never yet exceeded the income settled upon her by the Prince at the\r\ntime of their separation\xe2\x80\x94an income determined wholly by himself and his\r\nestimate of what was required to maintain the social consequence of his\r\nname, for which he had a boundless reverence. \xe2\x80\x9cShe thinks she\xe2\x80\x99s a model\r\nof thrift\xe2\x80\x94that she counts every shilling,\xe2\x80\x9d Madame Grandoni continued.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf there\xe2\x80\x99s a virtue she prides herself upon it\xe2\x80\x99s her economy. Indeed\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s the only thing for which she takes any credit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder if she knows that I\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he just hesitated, then went on\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cspend\r\nalmost nothing at all. But I\xe2\x80\x99d rather live on dry bread than that in a\r\ncountry like this, in this great English society, she shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t make a\r\nproper appearance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHer appearance is all you could wish. How can it help being proper\r\nwith me to set her off?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re the best thing she has, dear friend. So long as you\xe2\x80\x99re with her\r\nI feel a certain degree of security; and one of the things I came for\r\nwas to extract from you a promise that you won\xe2\x80\x99t leave her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, let us not tangle ourselves up with promises!\xe2\x80\x9d Madame Grandoni\r\nexclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cYou know the value of any engagement one may take with\r\nregard to the Princess; it\xe2\x80\x99s like promising you I\xe2\x80\x99ll stay in the\r\nbath when the hot water\xe2\x80\x99s on. When I begin to be scalded I\xe2\x80\x99ve to jump\r\nout\xe2\x80\x94naked as I may naturally be. I\xe2\x80\x99ll stay while I can, but I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nstay if she were to do certain things.\xe2\x80\x9d Madame Grandoni uttered\r\nthese last words with a clear emphasis, and for a minute she and her\r\ncompanion looked deep into each other\xe2\x80\x99s eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat things do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t say what things. It\xe2\x80\x99s utterly impossible to predict on any\r\noccasion what Christina will do. She\xe2\x80\x99s capable of giving us great\r\nsurprises. The things I mean are things I should recognise as soon as\r\nI saw them, and they would make me leave the house on the spot.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo that if you\xe2\x80\x99ve not left it yet\xe2\x80\x94?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked with extreme eagerness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s because I\xe2\x80\x99ve thought I may do some good by staying.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe seemed but half content with this answer; nevertheless he said in a\r\nmoment: \xe2\x80\x9cTo me it makes all the difference. And if anything of the kind\r\nyou speak of should happen, that would be only the greater reason for\r\nyour staying.\xe2\x80\x94You might interpose, you might arrest\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d He stopped short\r\nbefore her large Germanic grimace.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must have been in Rome more than once when the Tiber had\r\noverflowed, _\xc3\xa8 vero_? What would you have thought then if you had heard\r\npeople telling the poor wretches in the Ghetto, on the Ripetta, up to\r\ntheir knees in liquid mud, that they ought to interpose, to arrest?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Capisco bene_,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Prince, dropping his eyes. He appeared to\r\nhave closed them, for some moments, as if under a slow spasm of pain.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t tell you what torments me most,\xe2\x80\x9d he presently went on\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cthe\r\nthought that sometimes makes my heart rise into my mouth. It\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nhaunting fear.\xe2\x80\x9d And his pale face and disturbed respiration might\r\nindeed have been those of a man before whom some horrible spectre had\r\nrisen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou needn\xe2\x80\x99t tell me. I know what you mean, my poor friend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think then there _is_ a danger\xe2\x80\x94that she\xe2\x80\x99ll drag my name, do\r\nwhat no one has ever dared to do? That I\xe2\x80\x99d never forgive,\xe2\x80\x9d he declared\r\nalmost under his breath; and the hoarseness of his whisper lent it a\r\ngreat effect.\r\n\r\nMadame Grandoni hastily wondered if she had not better tell him (as it\r\nwould prepare him for the worst) that his wife cared about as much for\r\nhis name as for any old label on her luggage; but after an instant\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nreflexion she reserved this information for another hour. Besides, as\r\nshe said to herself, the Prince ought already to know perfectly to what\r\nextent Christina attached the idea of an obligation or an interdict to\r\nher ill-starred connexion with an ignorant and superstitious Italian\r\nrace whom she despised for their provinciality, their parsimony and\r\ntheir futility (she thought their talk the climax of childishness)\r\nand whose fatuous conception of their importance in the great modern\r\nworld she had on various public occasions sufficiently riddled with her\r\nderision. She finally contented herself with remarking: \xe2\x80\x9cDear Prince,\r\nyour wife\xe2\x80\x99s a very proud woman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, how could my wife be anything else? But her pride\xe2\x80\x99s not my pride.\r\nAnd she has such ideas; such opinions! Some of them are monstrous.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame Grandoni smiled. \xe2\x80\x9cShe doesn\xe2\x80\x99t think it so necessary to have them\r\nwhen you\xe2\x80\x99re not there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy then do you say that you enter into my fears\xe2\x80\x94that you recognise\r\nthe stories I\xe2\x80\x99ve heard?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI know not whether the good lady lost patience with his pressure;\r\nat all events she broke out with a certain sharpness. \xe2\x80\x9cUnderstand\r\nthis, understand this: Christina will never consider you\xe2\x80\x94your name,\r\nyour illustrious traditions\xe2\x80\x94in any case in which she doesn\xe2\x80\x99t consider\r\nherself much more!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Prince appeared to study for a moment this somewhat ambiguous yet\r\nportentous phrase; then he slowly got up with his hat in his hand and\r\nwalked about the room softly, solemnly, as if suffering from his long\r\nthin feet. He stopped before one of the windows and took another survey\r\nof South Street; then turning he suddenly asked in a voice into which\r\nhe had evidently endeavoured to infuse a colder curiosity: \xe2\x80\x9cIs she\r\nadmired in this place? Does she see many people?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s thought very strange of course. But she sees whom she likes. And\r\nthey mostly bore her to death!\xe2\x80\x9d Madame Grandoni conscientiously added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy then do you tell me this country pleases her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe old woman left her place. She had promised Christina, who detested\r\nthe sense of being under the same roof with her husband, that the\r\nlatter\xe2\x80\x99s visit should be kept within narrow limits; and this movement\r\nwas intended to signify as kindly as possible that it had better\r\nterminate. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s the common people who please her,\xe2\x80\x9d she returned with\r\nher hands folded on her crumpled satin stomach and her ancient eyes,\r\nstill keen for all comedy, raised to his face. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s the lower orders,\r\nthe _basso popolo_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe _basso popolo_?\xe2\x80\x9d The Prince stared at this fantastic announcement.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe _povera gente_,\xe2\x80\x9d pursued his friend, amused at his dismay.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe London mob\xe2\x80\x94the most horrible, the most brutal\xe2\x80\x94?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, she wishes to raise them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfter all, something like that\xe2\x80\x99s no more than I had heard,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nPrince gravely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Che vuole?_ Don\xe2\x80\x99t trouble yourself; it won\xe2\x80\x99t be for long!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame Grandoni saw this comforting assurance lost upon him; his\r\nface was turned to the door of the room, which had been thrown open,\r\nand all his attention given to the person who crossed the threshold.\r\nShe transferred her own to the same quarter and recognised the\r\nlittle artisan whom Christina had, in a manner so extraordinary and\r\nso profoundly characteristic, drawn into her box that night at the\r\ntheatre\xe2\x80\x94afterwards informing her old friend that she had sent for him\r\nto come and see her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Robinson!\xe2\x80\x9d the butler, who had had a lesson, announced in a loud\r\ncolourless tone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt won\xe2\x80\x99t be for long,\xe2\x80\x9d Madame Grandoni repeated for the Prince\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbenefit; but it was to Mr. Robinson the words had the air of being\r\naddressed.\r\n\r\nHyacinth stood, while she signalled to the servant to leave the door\r\nopen and wait, looking from the queer old lady, who was as queer as\r\nbefore, to the tall foreign gentleman (he recognised his foreignness at\r\na glance) whose eyes seemed to challenge him, to devour him; wondering\r\nif he had made some mistake and needing to remind himself that he had\r\nthe Princess\xe2\x80\x99s note in his pocket, with the day and hour as clear as\r\nher magnificent script could make them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-morning, good-morning. I hope you\xe2\x80\x99re well,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame Grandoni\r\nwith quick friendliness, but turning her back upon him at the same time\r\nin order to ask of their companion, in the other idiom, as she extended\r\nher hand: \xe2\x80\x9cAnd don\xe2\x80\x99t you leave London soon\xe2\x80\x94in a day or two?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Prince made no answer; he still scanned the little bookbinder\r\nfrom head to foot, as if wondering who the deuce he could be. His\r\neyes seemed to Hyacinth to search for the small neat bundle he ought\r\nto have had under his arm and without which he was incomplete. To the\r\nreader, however, it may be confided that, dressed more carefully than\r\nhe had ever been in his life before, stamped with that extraordinary\r\ntransformation which the British Sunday often operates in the person\r\nof the wage-earning cockney, with his handsome head uncovered and\r\nthe heat of wonder in his fine face, the young man from Lomax Place\r\nmight have passed for anything rather than a carrier of parcels. \xe2\x80\x9cThe\r\nPrincess wrote to me, madam, to come and see her,\xe2\x80\x9d he said as a prompt\r\nprecaution; in case he should have incurred the reproach of undue\r\nprecipitation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes, I daresay.\xe2\x80\x9d And Madame Grandoni guided the Prince to the door\r\nwith an expression of the desire he might have a comfortable journey\r\nback to Italy.\r\n\r\nBut he stood stiff there; he appeared to have jumped to a dark\r\nconclusion about Mr. Robinson. \xe2\x80\x9cI must see you once more. I must. It\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nimpossible\xe2\x80\x94!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh well, not in this house, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you do me the honour to meet me then?\xe2\x80\x9d And as the old lady\r\nhesitated he added with sudden intensity: \xe2\x80\x9cDearest friend, I beg you on\r\nmy knees!\xe2\x80\x9d After she had agreed that if he would write to her proposing\r\na day and place she would see him were it possible, he raised her\r\nancient knuckles to his lips and, without further notice of Hyacinth,\r\nturned away. She bade the servant announce the other visitor to the\r\nPrincess, and then approached Mr. Robinson, rubbing her hands and\r\nsmiling, her head very much to one side. He smiled back at her vaguely;\r\nhe didn\xe2\x80\x99t know what she might be going to say. What she said was, to\r\nhis surprise\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy poor young man, may I take the liberty of asking your age?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly, madam; I\xe2\x80\x99m twenty-four.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I hope you\xe2\x80\x99re industrious, and temperate in all ways and\xe2\x80\x94what do\r\nyou call it in English?\xe2\x80\x94steady.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think I\xe2\x80\x99m very wild,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hyacinth without offence. He\r\nthought the old woman patronising, but he forgave her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know how one speaks in this country to young men like you.\r\nPerhaps one\xe2\x80\x99s considered meddling or impertinent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI like the way you speak,\xe2\x80\x9d Hyacinth hastened to profess.\r\n\r\nShe stared, and then with a comical affectation of dignity: \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nvery good. I\xe2\x80\x99m glad it amuses you. You\xe2\x80\x99re evidently intelligent and\r\nclever,\xe2\x80\x9d she went on, \xe2\x80\x9cand if you\xe2\x80\x99re disappointed it will be a pity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you mean if I\xe2\x80\x99m disappointed?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I daresay you expect great things when you come into a house\r\nlike this. You must tell me if I upset you. I\xe2\x80\x99m very old-fashioned and\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99m not of this country. I speak as one speaks to young men like you in\r\nother places.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not so easily upset!\xe2\x80\x9d Hyacinth assured her with a flight of\r\nimagination. \xe2\x80\x9cTo expect anything one must know something, one must\r\nunderstand: isn\xe2\x80\x99t it so? And I\xe2\x80\x99m here without knowing, without\r\nunderstanding. I\xe2\x80\x99ve come only because a lady who seems to me very\r\nbeautiful and very kind has done me the honour to send for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame Grandoni examined him a moment as if struck by his good looks,\r\nby something delicate stamped on him everywhere. \xe2\x80\x9cI can see you\xe2\x80\x99re very\r\nclever, very intelligent; no, you\xe2\x80\x99re not like the young men I mean. All\r\nthe more reason\xe2\x80\x94!\xe2\x80\x9d And she paused, giving a short sigh. Her case might\r\nhave been all too difficult. \xe2\x80\x9cI want to warn you a little, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nknow how. If you were a young Roman it would be different.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA young Roman?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s where I live properly, in the Eternal City. If I hurt you, you\r\ncan explain it that way. No, you\xe2\x80\x99re not like them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t hurt me\xe2\x80\x94please believe that; you interest me very much,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Hyacinth, to whom it didn\xe2\x80\x99t occur that he himself might seem\r\npatronising. \xe2\x80\x9cOf what do you want to warn me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94only to advise you a little. Don\xe2\x80\x99t give up anything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat can I give up?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t give up _yourself_. I say that to you in your interest. I think\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ve some honest little trade\xe2\x80\x94I forget what. But whatever it may\r\nbe remember that to do it well is the best thing; better than paying\r\nextraordinary visits, better even than being liked by Princesses!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh yes, I see what you mean!\xe2\x80\x9d Hyacinth returned, exaggerating a\r\nlittle. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m very fond of my trade indeed, I assure you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m delighted to hear it. Hold fast to it then and be quiet; be\r\ndiligent and good and get on. I gathered the other night that you\xe2\x80\x99re\r\none of the young men who want everything changed\xe2\x80\x94I believe there are a\r\ngreat many in Italy and also in my own dear old Deutschland, and who\r\neven think it useful to throw bombs into innocent crowds and shoot\r\npistols at their rulers or at any one. I won\xe2\x80\x99t go into that. I might\r\nseem to be speaking for myself, and the fact is that for myself I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ncare; I\xe2\x80\x99m so old that I may hope to spend the few days that are left me\r\nwithout receiving a bullet. But before you go any further please think\r\na little whether you\xe2\x80\x99re right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt isn\xe2\x80\x99t just that you should impute to me ideas which I may not\r\nhave,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hyacinth, turning very red but taking more and more of a\r\nfancy, all the same, to Madame Grandoni. \xe2\x80\x9cYou talk at your ease about\r\nour ways and means, but if we were only to make use of those that you\r\nwould like to see\xe2\x80\x94!\xe2\x80\x9d And while he blushed, smiling, the young man shook\r\nhis head two or three times with great significance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t like to see any!\xe2\x80\x9d the old lady cried. \xe2\x80\x9cI like people to\r\nbear their troubles as one has done one\xe2\x80\x99s self. And as for injustice,\r\nyou see how kind I am to you when I say to you again, Don\xe2\x80\x99t, don\xe2\x80\x99t,\r\ngive anything up. I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell them to send you some tea,\xe2\x80\x9d she added as\r\nshe took her way out of the room, presenting to him her round, low,\r\naged back and dragging over the carpet a scanty and lustreless train.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXVII\r\n\r\n\r\nHe had been warned by Mr. Vetch as to what brilliant women might do\r\nwith him\xe2\x80\x94it was only a word on the old fiddler\xe2\x80\x99s lips, but the word\r\nhad had a point; he had been warned by Paul Muniment, and now he was\r\nadmonished by a person supremely well placed for knowing: a fact that\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t fail to deepen the emotion which, any time these three days,\r\nhad made him draw his breath more quickly. That emotion, nevertheless,\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t actually make him fear remote consequences; as he looked\r\nover the Princess Casamassima\xe2\x80\x99s drawing-room and inhaled an air that\r\nseemed to him inexpressibly delicate and sweet he hoped his adventure\r\nwould throw him on his mettle only half as much as the old lady had\r\nwished to intimate. He considered, one after the other, the different\r\nchairs, couches and ottomans the room contained\xe2\x80\x94he wished to treat\r\nhimself to the most sumptuous\xe2\x80\x94and then for reasons he knew best sank\r\ninto a seat covered with rose-coloured brocade and of which the legs\r\nand frame appeared of pure gold. Here he sat perfectly still, only\r\nwith his heart beating very sensibly and his eyes coursing again and\r\nagain from one object to another. The splendours and suggestions of\r\nCaptain Sholto\xe2\x80\x99s apartment were thrown completely into the shade by\r\nthe scene before him, and as the Princess didn\xe2\x80\x99t scruple to keep him\r\nwaiting twenty minutes (during which the butler came in and set out\r\non a small table a glittering tea-service) Hyacinth had time to count\r\nover the innumerable _bibelots_ (most of which he had never dreamed of)\r\ninvolved in the character of a woman of high fashion and to feel that\r\ntheir beauty and oddity revealed not only whole provinces of art, but\r\nrefinements of choice on the part of their owner, complications of mind\r\nand\xe2\x80\x94almost\xe2\x80\x94terrible depths of temperament.\r\n\r\nWhen at last the door opened and the servant, reappearing, threw it\r\nfar back as to make a wide passage for a person of the importance of\r\nhis mistress, Hyacinth\xe2\x80\x99s suspense became very acute; it was much the\r\nsame feeling with which, at the theatre, he had sometimes awaited\r\nthe entrance of a celebrated actress. In this case the actress was to\r\nperform for him alone. There was still a moment before she came on, and\r\nwhen she arrived she was so simply dressed\xe2\x80\x94besides his seeing her now\r\non her feet\xe2\x80\x94that she looked quite a different figure. She approached\r\nhim rapidly and a little stiffly and shyly, but in the prompt manner\r\nin which she shook hands was an evident desire to be very direct and\r\nperfectly easy. She might have been another person, but that person\r\nhad a beauty even more radiant; the fairness of her face shone forth at\r\nour young man as if to dissipate any doubts assailing and bewildering\r\nhim as to the reality of the vision bequeathed to him by his former\r\ninterview. And in this peculiar high grace of her presence he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nhave told you if she struck him as more proud or more kind.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve kept you a long time, but it\xe2\x80\x99s supposed not usually to be a\r\nbad place, my salon; there are various things to look at and perhaps\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ve noticed some of them. Over on that side for instance is rather\r\na curious collection of miniatures.\xe2\x80\x9d She spoke abruptly, quickly, as\r\nif conscious that their communion might be awkward and she were trying\r\nto strike instantly (to conjure that element away) the sort of note\r\nthat would make them both most comfortable. Quickly too she sat down\r\nbefore her tea-tray and poured him out a cup, which she handed him\r\nwithout asking if he would have it. He accepted it with a trembling\r\nhand, though he had no desire for it; he was too nervous to swallow\r\nthe tea, but it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have appeared to him possible to decline. When\r\nhe had murmured that he had indeed looked at all her things, but that\r\nit would take hours to do justice to such treasures, she asked if he\r\nwere fond of works of art; immediately adding, however, that she was\r\nafraid he had not many opportunities of seeing them, though of course\r\nthere were the public collections, open to all. He replied with perfect\r\nveracity that some of the happiest moments of his life had been spent\r\nat the British Museum and the National Gallery, and this fact appeared\r\nto interest her greatly, so that she straightway begged him to tell her\r\nwhat he thought of certain pictures and antiques. In this way it was\r\nthat in an incredibly short time, as appeared to him, he found himself\r\ndiscussing the Bacchus and Ariadne and the Elgin Marbles with one\r\nof the most remarkable women in Europe. It was true that she herself\r\ntalked most, passing precipitately from one point to another, putting\r\nquestions and not waiting for answers, describing and qualifying\r\nthings, expressing feelings, by the aid of phrases that he had never\r\nheard before but which seemed to him illuminating and happy\xe2\x80\x94as when\r\nfor instance she asked what art was, after all, but a synthesis made\r\nin the interest of pleasure, or said that she didn\xe2\x80\x99t like England in\r\nthe least, but absurdly loved it. It didn\xe2\x80\x99t occur to him to think these\r\ndiscriminations pedantic. Suddenly she threw off, \xe2\x80\x9cMadame Grandoni told\r\nme you saw my husband.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, was the gentleman your husband?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnfortunately! What do you think of him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I can\xe2\x80\x99t think\xe2\x80\x94!\xe2\x80\x9d Hyacinth decently pleaded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t either! I haven\xe2\x80\x99t seen him for nearly three years.\r\nHe wanted to see me to-day, but I refused.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94and the young man stared, not knowing how he ought to receive so\r\nunexpected a confidence. Then as the suggestions of inexperience are\r\nsometimes the happiest of all he spoke simply what was in his mind and\r\nsaid gently: \xe2\x80\x9cIt has made you\xe2\x80\x94naturally\xe2\x80\x94nervous.\xe2\x80\x9d Later on, when he had\r\nleft the house, he wondered how at that stage he could have ventured on\r\nsuch a familiar remark.\r\n\r\nBut she had taken it with a quick, surprised laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cHow do you know\r\nthat?\xe2\x80\x9d Before he had time to tell she added: \xe2\x80\x9cYour saying that\xe2\x80\x94that\r\nway\xe2\x80\x94shows me how right I was to ask you to come to see me. You know I\r\nhesitated. It shows me you\xe2\x80\x99ve perceptions; I guessed as much the other\r\nnight at the theatre. If I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have asked you. I may be\r\nwrong, but I like people who understand what one says to them, and also\r\nwhat one doesn\xe2\x80\x99t say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t think I understand too much. You might easily exaggerate that,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nHyacinth declared conscientiously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou confirm completely my first impression,\xe2\x80\x9d the Princess returned,\r\nsmiling in a way that showed him he really amused her. \xe2\x80\x9cWe shall\r\ndiscover the limits of your comprehension! I am atrociously nervous.\r\nBut it will pass. How\xe2\x80\x99s your cousin the dressmaker?\xe2\x80\x9d she inquired\r\nabruptly. And when Hyacinth had briefly given some account of poor\r\nPinnie\xe2\x80\x94described her as tolerably well for her, but old and tired\r\nand sad and not very successful\xe2\x80\x94she exclaimed impatiently, \xe2\x80\x9cAh, well,\r\nshe\xe2\x80\x99s not the only one!\xe2\x80\x9d and came back with irrelevance to the former\r\nquestion. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s not only my husband\xe2\x80\x99s visit\xe2\x80\x94absolutely unexpected!\xe2\x80\x94that\r\nhas made me fidgety, but the idea that now you\xe2\x80\x99ve been so kind as to\r\ncome here you may wonder why, after all, I made such a point of it,\r\nand even think any explanation I might be able to give you entirely\r\ninsufficient.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want any explanation,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hyacinth with a sense of great\r\npresence of mind.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s charming of you to say that, and I shall take you at your word.\r\nExplanations usually make things worse. All the same I don\xe2\x80\x99t want you\r\nto think (as you might have done so easily the other evening) that I\r\nwish only to treat you as a curious animal.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care how you treat me!\xe2\x80\x9d he smiled.\r\n\r\nThere was a considerable silence, after which she pursued: \xe2\x80\x9cAll I ask\r\nof my husband is to let me alone. But he won\xe2\x80\x99t. He won\xe2\x80\x99t return my\r\nindifference.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHyacinth wondered what reply he ought to make to such an announcement\r\nas that, and it seemed to him the least civility demanded was that he\r\nshould say\xe2\x80\x94as he could with such conviction\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cIt can\xe2\x80\x99t be easy to be\r\nindifferent to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not if I\xe2\x80\x99m odious? I _can_ be\xe2\x80\x94oh there\xe2\x80\x99s no doubt of that!\r\nHowever, I can honestly say that with the Prince I\xe2\x80\x99ve been exceedingly\r\nreasonable and that most of the wrongs\xe2\x80\x94the big ones, those that\r\nsettled the question\xe2\x80\x94have been on his side. You may tell me of course\r\nthat that\xe2\x80\x99s the pretension of every woman who has made a mess of her\r\nmarriage. But ask Madame Grandoni.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99ll tell me it\xe2\x80\x99s none of my business.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery true\xe2\x80\x94she might!\xe2\x80\x9d the Princess inconsequently laughed. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t know either why I should bore you with my domestic affairs;\r\nexcept that I\xe2\x80\x99ve been wondering what I could do to show you confidence\r\nin return for your showing so much in me. As this matter of my\r\nseparation from my husband happens to have been turned uppermost by his\r\nsudden descent on me I just mention it, though the subject\xe2\x80\x99s tiresome\r\nenough. Moreover, I ought to let you know that I\xe2\x80\x99ve very little respect\r\nfor distinctions of class\xe2\x80\x94the sort of thing they make so much of in\r\nthis country. They\xe2\x80\x99re doubtless convenient in some ways, but when one\r\nhas a reason\xe2\x80\x94a reason of feeling\xe2\x80\x94for overstepping them, and one allows\r\none\xe2\x80\x99s self to be deterred by some dreary superstition about one\xe2\x80\x99s place\r\nor some one else\xe2\x80\x99s place, then I think it\xe2\x80\x99s ignoble. It always belongs\r\nto one\xe2\x80\x99s place not to be a poor creature. I take it that if you\xe2\x80\x99re\r\na socialist you think about this as I do; but lest by chance, as the\r\nsense of those differences is the English religion, it may have rubbed\r\noff even on you (though I\xe2\x80\x99m more and more impressed with the fact that\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99re scarcely more British than I am): lest you should in spite of\r\nyour theoretic democracy be shocked at some of the applications that\r\nI, who cherish the creed, am capable of making of it, let me assure\r\nyou without delay that in that case we shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t get on together at\r\nall and had better part company before we go further.\xe2\x80\x9d She paused long\r\nenough for Hyacinth to declare with a great deal of emphasis that he\r\nwasn\xe2\x80\x99t easily shocked; and then restlessly, eagerly, as if it relieved\r\nher to talk and made their queer conjunction less abnormal that she\r\nshould talk most, she arrived at the point that she wanted to know\r\nthe _people_, and know them intimately\xe2\x80\x94the toilers and strugglers and\r\nsufferers\xe2\x80\x94because she was convinced they were the most interesting\r\nportion of society, and at the question, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat could really be in worse\r\ntaste than for me to carry into such an undertaking a pretension of\r\ngreater delicacy and finer manners? If I must do that,\xe2\x80\x9d she continued,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x99s simpler to leave them alone. But I can\xe2\x80\x99t leave them alone; they\r\npress on me, they haunt me, they fascinate me. There it is\xe2\x80\x94after all\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s very simple: I want to know them and I want you to help me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll help you with pleasure to the best of my humble ability. But\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ll be awfully disappointed,\xe2\x80\x9d Hyacinth said. Very strange it seemed\r\nto him that within so few days two ladies of rank should have found\r\noccasion to express to him the same mysterious longing. A breeze\r\nfrom a thoroughly unexpected quarter was indeed blowing through the\r\naristocracy. Nevertheless, though there was much of the same accent\r\nof passion in the Princess Casamassima\xe2\x80\x99s communication that there\r\nhad been in Lady Aurora\xe2\x80\x99s, and though he felt bound to discourage his\r\npresent interlocutress as he had done the other, the force that drove\r\nher struck him as a very different mixture from the shy, conscientious,\r\nanxious heresies of Rose Muniment\xe2\x80\x99s friend. The temper varied in the\r\ntwo women as much as the aspect and the address, and that perhaps made\r\ntheir curiosity the more significant.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI haven\xe2\x80\x99t the least doubt of it,\xe2\x80\x9d this investigator answered; \xe2\x80\x9cthere\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nnothing in life in which I\xe2\x80\x99ve not been awfully disappointed. But\r\ndisappointment for disappointment I shall like it better than some\r\nothers. You\xe2\x80\x99ll not persuade me either that among the people I speak\r\nof characters and passions and motives are not more natural, more\r\ncomplete, more _na\xc3\xaffs_. The upper classes are so deadly _banals_.\r\nMy husband traces his descent from the fifth century, and he\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\ngreatest bore in Europe. That\xe2\x80\x99s the kind of people I was condemned to\r\nby my marriage. Oh, if you knew what I\xe2\x80\x99ve been through you\xe2\x80\x99d allow\r\nthat intelligent mechanics (of course I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to know idiots)\r\nwould be a pleasant change. I must begin with some one\xe2\x80\x94mustn\xe2\x80\x99t I?\xe2\x80\x94so I\r\nbegan the other night with you!\xe2\x80\x9d As soon as she had uttered these words\r\nthe Princess added a correction with the consciousness of her mistake\r\nin her face. It made that face, to Hyacinth, more nobly, tenderly\r\nbeautiful. \xe2\x80\x9cThe only objection to you individually is that you\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nnothing of the people about you\xe2\x80\x94to-day not even the dress.\xe2\x80\x9d Her eyes\r\nwandered over him from head to foot, and their recognitions made him\r\nashamed. \xe2\x80\x9cI wish you had come in the clothes you wear at your work.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou see you do regard me as a curious animal,\xe2\x80\x9d he returned.\r\n\r\nIt was perhaps to contradict this that, after a moment, she began\r\nto tell him more about her domestic affairs. He ought to know who\r\nshe was, unless Captain Sholto had told him; and she mentioned her\r\nparentage\xe2\x80\x94American on the mother\xe2\x80\x99s side, Italian on the father\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nhow she had led from her youngest years a wandering Bohemian life in\r\na thousand different places (always in Europe, she had never been in\r\nAmerica and knew very little about it, though she wanted greatly to\r\ncross the Atlantic) and largely at one period in Rome. She had been\r\nmarried by her people, in a mercenary way, for the sake of a fortune\r\nand a great name, and it had turned out as badly as her worst enemy\r\ncould have wished. Her parents were dead, luckily for them, and she had\r\nno one near her of her own except Madame Grandoni, who belonged to her\r\nonly in the sense that she had known her as a girl; was an association\r\nof her\xe2\x80\x94what should she call them?\xe2\x80\x94her uneasy but innocent years. Not\r\nthat she had ever been very innocent; she had had a horrible education.\r\nHowever, she had known a few good people\xe2\x80\x94people she respected then;\r\nbut Madame Grandoni was the only one who had stuck to her. She too was\r\nliable to leave her any day; the Princess appeared to intimate that her\r\ndestiny might require her to take some step which would test severely\r\nthe old woman\xe2\x80\x99s attachment. It would detain her too long to make him\r\nunderstand the stages by which she had arrived at her present state of\r\nmind: her disgust with a thousand social arrangements, her rebellion\r\nagainst the selfishness, the corruption, the iniquity, the cruelty,\r\nthe imbecility of the people who all over Europe had the upper hand.\r\nIf he could have seen her life, the _milieu_ in which she had for\r\nseveral years been condemned to move, the evolution of her opinions\r\n(Hyacinth was delighted to hear her use that term) would strike him\r\nas perfectly logical. She had been humiliated, outraged, tortured; she\r\nconsidered that she too was one of the numerous class who could be put\r\non a tolerable footing only by a revolution. At any rate she had some\r\nself-respect left, and there was still more that she wanted to recover;\r\nthe only way to arrive at which was to throw herself into some effort\r\nthat would make her forget her own affairs and comprehend the troubles\r\nand efforts of others. Hyacinth listened to her with a wonderment\r\nwhich, as she went on, was transformed into willing submission; she\r\nseemed so natural, so vivid, so exquisitely generous and sincere. By\r\nthe time he had been with her half an hour she had made the situation\r\nitself easy and usual, and a third person who should have joined them\r\nat this moment would have noticed nothing to suggest that friendly\r\nsocial intercourse between little bookbinders and Neapolitan princesses\r\nwas not in London a matter of daily occurrence.\r\n\r\nHyacinth had seen plenty of women who chattered about themselves and\r\ntheir affairs\xe2\x80\x94a vulgar garrulity of confidence was indeed a leading\r\ncharacteristic of the sex as he had hitherto learned to know it\xe2\x80\x94but\r\nhe was quick to perceive that the great lady who now took the trouble\r\nto open herself to him was not of a gossiping habit; that she must be\r\non the contrary, as a general thing, proudly, ironically reserved,\r\neven to the point of passing with many people for a model of the\r\nunsatisfactory. It was very possible she was capricious; yet the fact\r\nthat her present sympathies and curiosities might be a caprice wore\r\nin her visitor\xe2\x80\x99s eyes no sinister aspect. Why was it not a noble and\r\ninteresting whim, and why mightn\xe2\x80\x99t he stand for the hour at any rate\r\nin the silvery moonshine it cast on his path? It must be added that he\r\nwas far from taking in everything she said, some of her allusions and\r\nimplications being so difficult to seize that they mainly served to\r\nreveal to him the limits of his own acquaintance with life. Her words\r\nevoked all sorts of shadowy suggestions of things he was condemned not\r\nto know, touching him most when he had not the key to them. This was\r\nespecially the case with her reference to her career in Italy, on her\r\nhusband\xe2\x80\x99s estates, and her relations with his family, who considered\r\nthat they had done her a great honour in receiving her into their\r\naugust circle (putting the best face on a bad business) after they\r\nhad moved heaven and earth to keep her out of it. The position made\r\nfor her among such people and what she had had to suffer from their\r\nfamily tone, their opinions and customs (though what these might be\r\nremained vague to her listener) had evidently planted in her soul\r\na lasting resentment and contempt; and Hyacinth gathered that the\r\nforce of reaction and revenge might carry her far, make her modern\r\nand democratic and heretical _\xc3\xa0 outrance_\xe2\x80\x94lead her to swear by Darwin\r\nand Spencer and all the scientific iconoclasts as well as by the\r\nrevolutionary spirit. He surely needn\xe2\x80\x99t have been so sensible of the\r\nweak spots in his comprehension of the Princess when he could already\r\nsurmise that personal passion had counted for so much in the formation\r\nof her views. This induction, however, which had no harshness, didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nmake her affect him any the less as a creature compounded of the\r\nfinest elements; brilliant, delicate, complicated, but complicated with\r\nsomething divine.\r\n\r\nIt was not till after he had left her that he became conscious she\r\nhad forced him to talk in spite of talking so much herself. He drew\r\na long breath as he reflected that he hadn\xe2\x80\x99t made quite such an ass\r\nof himself as might very well have happened; he had been saved by\r\nthe thrill of his interest and admiration, which had not gone to his\r\nhead and prompted him to show that he too in his improbable little\r\nway was remarkable, but had kept him in a state of anxious, conscious\r\ntension, as if the occasion had been a great appointed solemnity,\r\nsome initiation more formal than any he believed practised even in the\r\ngrimmest subterranean circles. He had said indeed much more than he had\r\nwarrant for when she questioned him on his \xe2\x80\x9cradical\xe2\x80\x9d affiliations; he\r\nhad spoken as if the movement were vast and mature, whereas in fact, so\r\nfar at least as he was as yet concerned with it and could answer for it\r\nfrom personal knowledge, it was circumscribed by the hideously-papered\r\nwalls of the little club-room at the \xe2\x80\x9cSun and Moon.\xe2\x80\x9d He reproached\r\nhimself with this laxity, but it had not been engendered by pride. He\r\nwas only afraid of disappointing his hostess too much, of making her\r\nsay, \xe2\x80\x9cWhy in the world then did you come to see me if you\xe2\x80\x99ve nothing\r\nmore remarkable to put before me?\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94a question to which of course he\r\nwould have had an answer ready but for its being so impossible to say\r\nhe had never asked to come and that his coming was her own affair. He\r\nwanted too much to come a second time to have the courage to make that\r\nspeech. Nevertheless when she exclaimed, changing the subject abruptly,\r\nas she always did, from something else they had been talking about,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder if I shall ever see you again!\xe2\x80\x9d he replied with perfect\r\nsincerity that it was scarce possible for him to believe anything so\r\ndelightful could be repeated. There were some kinds of happiness that\r\nto many people never came at all, and to others could come only once.\r\nHe added: \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s very true I had just that feeling after I left you the\r\nother night at the theatre. And yet here I am!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, there you are,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Princess thoughtfully\xe2\x80\x94as if this might\r\nbe a still graver and more embarrassing fact than she had yet supposed\r\nit. \xe2\x80\x9cI take it there\xe2\x80\x99s nothing essentially inconceivable in my seeing\r\nyou again; but it may very well be that you\xe2\x80\x99ll never again find it\r\nso pleasant. Perhaps that\xe2\x80\x99s the happiness that comes but once. At any\r\nrate, you know, I\xe2\x80\x99m going away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes, of course; every one leaves town\xe2\x80\x94!\xe2\x80\x9d Hyacinth rose to that\r\noccasion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo _you_, Mr. Robinson?\xe2\x80\x9d the Princess asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I don\xe2\x80\x99t as a general thing.
