I should like\r\nthe v\xe2\x80\x99ice of the company on that question.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen the tone fell as low as this Paul Muniment held his peace save for\r\nwhistling a little and leaning back with his hands in his pockets and\r\nhis eyes on the table. Hyacinth often supposed him to be on the point\r\nof breaking out and letting the company know what he thought of them\xe2\x80\x94he\r\nhad a perfectly clear vision of what he must think: but Muniment never\r\ncompromised his popularity to that degree; he judged it\xe2\x80\x94this he once\r\ntold his young comrade\xe2\x80\x94too valuable a weapon, so that he cultivated the\r\nfaculty of patience, which had the advantage of showing one more and\r\nmore that one must do one\xe2\x80\x99s thinking for one\xe2\x80\x99s self. His popularity\r\nindeed struck Hyacinth as rather an uncertain amount, and the only\r\nmistake he had seen a symptom of on his friend\xe2\x80\x99s part was a tendency\r\nto overestimate it. Muniment thought many of their colleagues asinine,\r\nbut it was Hyacinth\xe2\x80\x99s belief that he himself knew still better how\r\nasinine they were; and this inadequate conception supported in some\r\ndegree on Paul\xe2\x80\x99s part his theory of his influence\xe2\x80\x94an influence that\r\nwould be stronger than any other on the day he should choose to exert\r\nit. Hyacinth only wished that day would come; it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be till\r\nthen, he was sure, that they would all know where they were and that\r\nthe good they were striving for, blindly, obstructedly, in a kind of\r\neternal dirty intellectual fog, would pass from the stage of crude\r\ndiscussion and mere sore, sharp, tantalising desirableness into that\r\nof solid, seated reality. Muniment was listened to unanimously when\r\nhe spoke and much talked about, usually with a knowing, implicit\r\nallusiveness, when he was absent; it was generally admitted he could\r\nsee further than most. But it was suspected he wanted to see further\r\nthan was necessary; as one of the most inveterate frequenters of\r\nthe club remarked one evening, if a man could see as far as he could\r\n\xe2\x80\x99eave a brick this was far enough. There was an idea he had nothing\r\nparticular to complain of personally, or perhaps that if he had he\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t complain of it\xe2\x80\x94an attitude which could only contain the germs\r\nof a latent disaffection. Hyacinth was aware of being himself exposed\r\nto the same imputation; but he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help it\xe2\x80\x94it would have been\r\nimpossible to him to keep up his character for sincerity by revealing\r\nat the \xe2\x80\x9cSun and Moon\xe2\x80\x9d the condition of his wardrobe or by announcing\r\nthat he hadn\xe2\x80\x99t had a penn\xe2\x80\x99orth of bacon for six months. There were\r\nmembers of the club who were apparently always in the enjoyment of\r\ninvoluntary leisure\xe2\x80\x94narrating the vainest peregrinations in search of a\r\njob, the cruellest rebuffs, the most vivid anecdotes of the insolence\r\nof office. They made Hyacinth uncomfortably conscious at times that\r\nif _he_ should be out of work it would be wholly by his own fault;\r\nthat he held in his hand a fine bread-winning tool on which he might\r\nabsolutely count. He was also not unadvised, however, that his position\r\nin this little band of malcontents (it was small only if measured\r\nby the numbers gathered on any one occasion; he liked to think it\r\nlarge in its latent possibilities, its mysterious ramifications and\r\naffiliations) was peculiar and distinguished: it would be favourable\r\nif he should develop the kind of energy and assurance that would help\r\nhim to make use of it. He had an intimate conviction\xe2\x80\x94the proof of it\r\nwas in the air, in the sensible facility of his footing at the \xe2\x80\x9cSun\r\nand Moon\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94that Eustache Poupin had taken on himself to disseminate the\r\nanecdote of his origin, of his mother\xe2\x80\x99s disaster; in consequence of\r\nwhich, as the victim of social infamy, of heinous laws, it was conceded\r\nto him that he had a larger account to settle even than most. He was\r\n_ab ovo_ a revolutionist, and that balanced against his smart neckties,\r\na certain suspicious security that was perceived in him as to the _h_\r\n(he had had from his earliest years a natural command of it) and the\r\nfact that he possessed the sort of hand on which there is always a\r\npremium\xe2\x80\x94an accident somehow to be guarded against in a thorough-going\r\nsystem of equality. He never challenged Poupin on the subject, for he\r\nowed the Frenchman too much to reproach him with any officious step\r\nthat was meant in kindness; and, moreover, his fellow-labourer at old\r\nCrook\xe2\x80\x99s had said to him, as if to anticipate such an impugnment of\r\nhis discretion: \xe2\x80\x9cRemember, my child, that I\xe2\x80\x99m incapable of drawing\r\naside any veil that you may have preferred to drop over your lacerated\r\npersonality. Your moral dignity will always be safe with me. But\r\nremember at the same time that among the disinherited there\xe2\x80\x99s a mystic\r\nlanguage which dispenses with proofs\xe2\x80\x94a freemasonry, a reciprocal\r\ndivination: they understand each other at half a word.\xe2\x80\x9d It was at half\r\na word then in Bloomsbury that Hyacinth had been understood; but there\r\nwas a certain delicacy in him that forbade him to push his advantage,\r\nto treat implications of sympathy, none the less definite for being\r\nawkward and obscure, as steps in the ladder of success. He had no wish\r\nto be a leader because his mother had murdered her lover and died\r\nin penal servitude: these circumstances recommended intentness, but\r\nthey also imposed modesty. When the gathering at the \xe2\x80\x9cSun and Moon\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nwas at its best and its temper seemed really an earnest of what was\r\nthe basis of all its calculations\xe2\x80\x94that the people was only a sleeping\r\nlion, already breathing shorter and beginning to stretch its limbs\r\nand stiffen its claws\xe2\x80\x94at these hours, some of them thrilling enough,\r\nHyacinth waited for the voice that should allot him the particular part\r\nhe was to play. His ambition was to play it with brilliancy, to offer\r\nan example\xe2\x80\x94an example even that might survive him\xe2\x80\x94of pure youthful,\r\nalmost juvenile, consecration. He was conscious of no commission to\r\ngive the promises, to assume the responsibilities, of a redeemer, and\r\nhe had no envy of the man on whom this burden should rest. Muniment\r\nindeed might carry it, and it was the first article of his faith\r\nthat to help him to carry it the better he himself was ready for any\r\nsacrifice. Then it was\xe2\x80\x94on these nights of intenser vibration\xe2\x80\x94that he\r\nwaited for the sacred sign.\r\n\r\nThey came oftener this second winter, for the season was terribly\r\nhard; and as in that lower world one walked with one\xe2\x80\x99s ear nearer the\r\nground the deep perpetual groan of London misery seemed to swell and\r\nswell and form the whole undertone of life. The filthy air reached\r\nthe place in the damp coats of silent men and hung there till it was\r\nbrewed to a nauseous warmth, and ugly serious faces squared themselves\r\nthrough it, and strong-smelling pipes contributed their element in a\r\nfierce dogged manner which appeared to say that it now had to stand\r\nfor everything\xe2\x80\x94for bread and meat and beer, for shoes and blankets\r\nand the poor things at the pawnbroker\xe2\x80\x99s and the smokeless chimney at\r\nhome. Hyacinth\xe2\x80\x99s colleagues affected him as wiser then, as more richly\r\npermeated with intentions boding ill to the satisfied classes; and\r\nthough the note of popularity was still most effectively struck by\r\nthe man who could demand oftenest, unpractically, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat the hell am I\r\nto do with half a quid?\xe2\x80\x9d it was brought home to our hero on more than\r\none occasion that revolution was ripe at last. This was especially\r\nthe case on the evening I began by referring to, when Eustache Poupin\r\nsqueezed in and announced, as if it were a great piece of news, that\r\nin the east of London that night there were forty thousand men out of\r\nwork. He looked round the circle with his dilated foreign eye as he\r\ntook his place: he seemed to address the company individually as well\r\nas collectively and to make each man responsible for hearing him. He\r\nowed his position at the \xe2\x80\x9cSun and Moon\xe2\x80\x9d to the brilliancy with which\r\nhe represented the political exile, the magnanimous immaculate citizen\r\nwrenched out of bed at dead of night, torn from his hearthstone, his\r\nloved ones and his profession and hurried across the frontier with only\r\nthe coat on his back. Poupin had performed in this character now for\r\nmany years, but had never lost the bloom of the outraged proscript,\r\nand the passionate pictures he had often drawn of the bitterness of\r\nexile were moving even to those who knew with what success he had set\r\nup his household gods in Lisson Grove. He was recognised as suffering\r\neverything for his opinions; and his hearers in Bloomsbury, who even\r\nin their most infuriated hours felt as Britons, appeared never to have\r\nmade the subtle reflexion, though they made many others, that there\r\nwas a want of tact in his calling on them to sympathise with him for\r\nbeing one of themselves. He imposed himself by the eloquence of his\r\nassumption that if one were not in the beautiful supreme France one was\r\nnowhere worth speaking of, and ended by producing an impression that\r\nthat country had a quite supernatural charm. Muniment had once said\r\nto Hyacinth that he was sure Poupin would be very sorry to be enabled\r\nto go home again (as he really might from one week to the other,\r\nthe Republic being so indulgent and the amnesty to the Communards\r\nconstantly extended) for over there he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t be a refugee; and\r\nhowever this might be he certainly flourished a good deal in London on\r\nthe basis of this very fact that he so suffered from it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you tell us that as if it was so very striking? Don\xe2\x80\x99t we know\r\nit and haven\xe2\x80\x99t we known it always? But you\xe2\x80\x99re right; we behave as if\r\nwe knew nothing at all,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Schinkel, the German cabinet-maker\r\nwho had originally introduced Captain Sholto to the \xe2\x80\x9cSun and Moon.\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\nhad a long, unhealthy, benevolent face and greasy hair, and constantly\r\nwore an untidy bandage round his neck, as if for a local ailment. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nremind us\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s very well; but we shall forget it in half an hour.\r\nWe\xe2\x80\x99re not serious.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Pardon, pardon_; for myself I don\xe2\x80\x99t admit that!\xe2\x80\x9d Poupin replied,\r\nstriking the table with his finger-tips several times, very fast. \xe2\x80\x9cIf\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99m not serious I\xe2\x80\x99m nothing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no, you\xe2\x80\x99re something,\xe2\x80\x9d said the German, smoking his monumental pipe\r\nwith a contemplative air. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re all something, but I\xe2\x80\x99m not sure it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nanything very useful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, things would be worse without us. I\xe2\x80\x99d jolly rather be in\r\nhere, in _this_ kind of muck, than outside,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked the fat man who\r\nunderstood dogs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly, it\xe2\x80\x99s very pleasant, especially if you\xe2\x80\x99ve your beer; but\r\nnot so pleasant over there at the Docks, where fifty thousand people\r\nstarve. It\xe2\x80\x99s a very unpleasant night,\xe2\x80\x9d the cabinet-maker went on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can it be worse?\xe2\x80\x9d Eustache Poupin asked while he looked at the\r\nGerman as to make him responsible for the fat man\xe2\x80\x99s reflexion. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s so\r\nbad that the imagination recoils, refuses\xe2\x80\x94!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, we don\xe2\x80\x99t care for the imagination!\xe2\x80\x9d the fat man declared. \xe2\x80\x9cWe want\r\na compact body in marching order.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you call a compact body?\xe2\x80\x9d the little grey-faced shoemaker\r\ndemanded. \xe2\x80\x9cI daresay you don\xe2\x80\x99t mean your kind of body.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I know what I mean,\xe2\x80\x9d said the fat man severely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a grand thing. Perhaps one of these days you\xe2\x80\x99ll tell us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll see it for yourself perhaps, before that day comes,\xe2\x80\x9d the\r\ngentleman with the silver ring rejoined. \xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps when you do you\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nremember.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you know, Schinkel says we don\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said the shoemaker, nodding\r\nat the cloud-compelling German.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care a bloody rap what no man says!\xe2\x80\x9d the dog-fancier\r\nexclaimed, gazing straight before him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey say it\xe2\x80\x99s a bad year\xe2\x80\x94the blockheads in the newspapers,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr.\r\nSchinkel went on, addressing himself to the company at large. \xe2\x80\x9cThey say\r\nthat on purpose\xe2\x80\x94to convey the impression that there are such things as\r\ngood years. I ask the company, has any gentleman present ever happened\r\nto notice that article? The good year\xe2\x80\x99s yet to come: it might begin\r\nto-night, if we like: it all depends on our being able to be serious\r\nfor a few hours. But that\xe2\x80\x99s too much to expect. Mr. Muniment\xe2\x80\x99s very\r\nserious; he looks as if he was waiting for the signal, but he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nspeak\xe2\x80\x94he never speaks if I want particularly to hear him. He only\r\ndeliberates very deeply\xe2\x80\x94oh I\xe2\x80\x99m sure. But it\xe2\x80\x99s almost as bad to think\r\nwithout speaking as to speak without thinking.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHyacinth always admired the cool, easy way in which Muniment comported\r\nhimself when the attention of the public was directed at him. These\r\nmanifestations of curiosity or of hostility would have put him out\r\nimmensely himself. When a lot of people, especially the kind of people\r\ncollected at the \xe2\x80\x9cSun and Moon,\xe2\x80\x9d looked at him or listened to him all\r\nat once, he always blushed and stammered, feeling that if he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nhave a million of spectators (which would have been inspiring) he\r\nshould prefer to have but two or three; there was something rather\r\nawful in twenty.\r\n\r\nMuniment smiled an instant good-humouredly; then after a moment\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhesitation, looking across at the German and the German only, as if his\r\nremark were worth noticing but it didn\xe2\x80\x99t matter if the others didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nunderstand the reply, he said simply: \xe2\x80\x9cHoffendahl\xe2\x80\x99s in London.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHoffendahl? _Gott in Himmel!_\xe2\x80\x9d the cabinet-maker exclaimed, taking the\r\npipe out of his mouth. And the two men exchanged a longish glance. Then\r\nMr. Schinkel remarked: \xe2\x80\x9cThat surprises me, _sehr_. Are you very sure?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMuniment continued for a little to look at him. \xe2\x80\x9cIf I keep quiet half\r\nan hour, with so many valuable suggestions flying all round me, you\r\nthink I say too little. Then if I open my head to give out three words\r\nyou appear to think I say too much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh no, on the contrary\xe2\x80\x94I want you to say three more. If you tell me\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ve seen him I shall be perfectly satisfied.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUpon my word I should hope so! Do you think he\xe2\x80\x99s the kind of bloke a\r\nfellow says he has seen?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, when he hasn\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d said Eustache Poupin, who had been listening.\r\nEvery one was listening now.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt depends on the fellow he says it to. Not even here?\xe2\x80\x9d the German\r\nasked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh here!\xe2\x80\x9d Paul Muniment exclaimed in a peculiar tone while he resumed\r\nhis muffled whistle again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake care\xe2\x80\x94take care; you\xe2\x80\x99ll make me think you haven\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Poupin\r\nwith his excited expression.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s just what I want,\xe2\x80\x9d said Muniment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Nun_, I understand,\xe2\x80\x9d the cabinet-maker remarked, restoring his pipe\r\nto his lips after an interval almost as momentous as the stoppage of a\r\nsteamer in mid-ocean.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_\xe2\x80\x99Ere_, \xe2\x80\x99ere?\xe2\x80\x9d repeated the small shoemaker indignantly. \xe2\x80\x9cI daresay\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s as good as the place he came from. He might look in and see what\r\nhe thinks of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a place you might tell us a little about now,\xe2\x80\x9d the fat man\r\nsuggested as if he had been waiting for his chance.\r\n\r\nBefore the shoemaker had time to notice this challenge some one\r\ninquired with a hoarse petulance who the bloody blazes they were\r\ntalking about; and Mr. Schinkel took upon himself to reply that they\r\nwere talking about a man who hadn\xe2\x80\x99t done what he had done by simply\r\nexchanging abstract ideas, however valuable, with his friends in a\r\nrespectable pot-house.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat the devil has he done then?\xe2\x80\x9d some one else demanded; and Muniment\r\nreplied quietly that he had spent twelve years in a Prussian prison\r\nand was consequently still an object of a good deal of interest to the\r\npolice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, if you call that very useful I must say I prefer a pot-house!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\ncried the shoemaker, appealing to all the company and looking, as it\r\nappeared to Hyacinth, particularly hideous.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Doch, doch_, it\xe2\x80\x99s useful,\xe2\x80\x9d the German remarked philosophically among\r\nhis yellow clouds.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean to say you\xe2\x80\x99re not prepared for that yourself?\xe2\x80\x9d Muniment\r\nasked of the shoemaker.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPrepared for that? I thought we were going to smash that sort of shop\r\naltogether; I thought that was the main part of the job.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99ll smash best who\xe2\x80\x99ve been inside,\xe2\x80\x9d the German said; \xe2\x80\x9cunless\r\nthey\xe2\x80\x99ve only gone bad, like fish too long caught. But Hoffendahl\xe2\x80\x99s all\r\nthere yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh no; no smashing, no smashing of any valuable property,\xe2\x80\x9d Muniment\r\nwent on. \xe2\x80\x9cThere are no wrong places\xe2\x80\x94there are only wrong uses for them.\r\nWe want to keep them standing and even to put up a few more; but the\r\ndifference will be that we shall put the correct sort into them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI take your idea\xe2\x80\x94that Griffin\xe2\x80\x99s one of the correct sort,\xe2\x80\x9d the fat man\r\nremarked, indicating the shoemaker.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought we was going to \xe2\x80\x99ave their \xe2\x80\x99eads\xe2\x80\x94all that bloomin\xe2\x80\x99 lot!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nMr. Griffin protested; while Eustache Poupin began to enlighten the\r\ncompany as to the great Hoffendahl, one of the purest martyrs of their\r\ncause, a man who had been through everything\xe2\x80\x94who had been scarred and\r\nbranded, tortured, almost flayed, and had never given his would-be\r\nbutchers the names they wanted. Was it possible they didn\xe2\x80\x99t remember\r\nthat great combined assault, early in the sixties, which took place in\r\nfour Continental cities at once and which in spite of every effort to\r\nsmother it up\xe2\x80\x94there had been editors and journalists transported even\r\nfor hinting at it\xe2\x80\x94had done more for the social question than anything\r\nbefore or since? \xe2\x80\x9cThrough \xe2\x80\x99im being served in the manner you describe?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsome one asked with plainness; to which Poupin replied that it was one\r\nof those failures that are more glorious than any success. Muniment\r\nsaid that the affair had been only a flash in the pan, but that the\r\ngreat value of it was this\xe2\x80\x94that whereas some forty persons (and of\r\nboth sexes) had been engaged in it, only one had been seized and had\r\nsuffered. It had been Hoffendahl himself who was collared. Certainly\r\nhe had suffered much, he had suffered for every one; but from that\r\npoint of view\xe2\x80\x94that of the economy of material\xe2\x80\x94the thing had been a rare\r\nsuccess.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you know what I call the others? I call \xe2\x80\x99em bloody sneaks!\xe2\x80\x9d the\r\nfat man cried; and Eustache Poupin, turning to Muniment, expressed\r\nthe hope that he didn\xe2\x80\x99t really approve of such a solution\xe2\x80\x94didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nconsider that an economy of heroism was an advantage to any cause.\r\nHe himself esteemed Hoffendahl\xe2\x80\x99s attempt because it had shaken, more\r\nthan anything\xe2\x80\x94except of course the Commune\xe2\x80\x94had shaken it since the\r\nFrench Revolution, the rotten fabric of the actual social order, and\r\nbecause that very fact of the impunity, the invisibility of the persons\r\nconcerned in it had given the predatory classes, had given all Europe,\r\na shudder that had not yet subsided; but for his part, he must regret\r\nthat some of the associates of the devoted victim had not come forward\r\nand insisted on sharing with him his tortures and his captivity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_\xc3\x87\xe2\x80\x99aurait \xc3\xa9t\xc3\xa9 d\xe2\x80\x99un bel exemple!_\xe2\x80\x9d said the Frenchman with an\r\nimpressive moderation of statement which made even those who\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t understand him see he was saying something fine; while the\r\ncabinet-maker observed that in Hoffendahl\xe2\x80\x99s place any of them would\r\nhave stood out just the same. He didn\xe2\x80\x99t care if they set it down to\r\nself-love (Mr. Schinkel called it \xe2\x80\x9cloaf\xe2\x80\x9d) but he might say that he\r\nhimself would have done so if he had been trusted and had been bagged.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want to have it all drawn up clear first; then I\xe2\x80\x99ll go in,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nfat man, who seemed to think it was expected of him to be reassuring.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, who the dickens is to draw it up, eh? That\xe2\x80\x99s what we happen to\r\nbe talking about,\xe2\x80\x9d returned his antagonist the shoemaker.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA fine example, old man? Is that your idea of a fine example?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nMuniment, with his amused face, asked of Poupin. \xe2\x80\x9cA fine example of\r\nasininity! Are there capable people, in such plenty, about the place?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCapable of greatness of soul, I grant you not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour greatness of soul is usually greatness of blundering. A man\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nforemost duty is not to get collared. If you want to show you\xe2\x80\x99re\r\ncapable, that\xe2\x80\x99s the way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt this Hyacinth suddenly felt himself moved to speak. \xe2\x80\x9cBut some one\r\nmust be caught always, must he not? Hasn\xe2\x80\x99t some one always been?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I daresay you\xe2\x80\x99ll be if you like it!\xe2\x80\x9d Muniment replied without\r\nlooking at him. \xe2\x80\x9cIf they succeed in potting you, do as Hoffendahl\r\ndid, and do it as a matter of course; but if they don\xe2\x80\x99t, make it your\r\nsupreme duty, make it your religion, to lie close and keep yourself for\r\nanother go. The world\xe2\x80\x99s full of unclean beasts whom I shall be glad to\r\nsee shovelled away by the thousand; but when it\xe2\x80\x99s a question of honest\r\nmen and men of courage I protest against the idea that two should be\r\nsacrificed where one will serve.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Trop d\xe2\x80\x99arithm\xc3\xa9tique\xe2\x80\x94trop d\xe2\x80\x99arithm\xc3\xa9tique!_\xe2\x80\x94That\xe2\x80\x99s fearfully English!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nPoupin cried.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo doubt, no doubt; what else should it be? You shall never share my\r\nfate if I have a fate and I can prevent it!\xe2\x80\x9d Muniment laughed.\r\n\r\nPoupin stared at him and his coarse mirth, as if he thought the English\r\nfrivolous as well as calculating; then he rejoined: \xe2\x80\x9cIf I suffer I\r\ntrust it may be for suffering humanity, but I trust it may also be for\r\nFrance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I hope you ain\xe2\x80\x99t going to suffer any more for France,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr.\r\nGriffin. \xe2\x80\x9cHasn\xe2\x80\x99t it done that insatiable old country of yours some good\r\nby this time, all you\xe2\x80\x99ve had to put up with?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I want to know what Hoffendahl has come over for; it\xe2\x80\x99s very\r\nkind of him, I\xe2\x80\x99m sure. What\xe2\x80\x99s he going to do for _us_?\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s what _I_\r\nwant to know,\xe2\x80\x9d brought out in a loud argumentative tone a personage at\r\nthe end of the table most distant from Muniment\xe2\x80\x99s place. His name was\r\nDelancey and he gave himself out as holding a position in a manufactory\r\nof soda-water; but Hyacinth had a secret belief that he was really a\r\nhairdresser\xe2\x80\x94a belief connected with a high lustrous curl or crest which\r\nhe wore on the summit of his large head, as well as with the manner in\r\nwhich he thrust over his ear, as if it were a barber\xe2\x80\x99s comb, the pencil\r\naddressed to his careful note-taking on the discussions conducted\r\nat the \xe2\x80\x9cSun and Moon.\xe2\x80\x9d His opinions were distinct and frequently\r\nexpressed; he had a watery (Muniment had once called it a soda-watery)\r\neye and a personal aversion to a lord. He desired to change everything\r\nexcept religion, of which he approved.\r\n\r\nMuniment answered that he was unable to say as yet what the German\r\nrevolutionist had come to England for, but that he hoped to be able to\r\ngive some information on the matter the next time they should meet.\r\nIt was very certain Hoffendahl hadn\xe2\x80\x99t come for nothing, and he would\r\nundertake to declare that they would all feel within a short time that\r\nhe had given a lift to the cause they had at heart. He had had a great\r\nexperience, which they might very well find it useful to appeal to.\r\nIf there was a way for them then and there he would be sure to know\r\nthe way. \xe2\x80\x9cI quite agree with the majority of you\xe2\x80\x94as I take it to be,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nMuniment went on in his fresh, cheerful, reasonable manner\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI quite\r\nagree with you that the time has come to settle upon it and to follow\r\nit. I quite agree with you that the actual state of things is\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he\r\npaused a moment and then went on in the same pleasant tone\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cis infamous\r\nand hellish.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThese remarks were received with a differing demonstration: some of\r\nthe company declaring that if the Dutchman cared to come round and\r\nsmoke a pipe they\xe2\x80\x99d be glad to see him\xe2\x80\x94perhaps he\xe2\x80\x99d show where the\r\nthumbscrews had been put on; others being strongly of the opinion that\r\nthey didn\xe2\x80\x99t want any more advice\xe2\x80\x94they had already had advice enough\r\nto turn a donkey\xe2\x80\x99s stomach. What they wanted was to put forth their\r\nmight without any more palaver; to do for something or for some one;\r\nto go out somewhere and smash something on the spot\xe2\x80\x94why not?\xe2\x80\x94smash\r\nit that very night. While they sat still and talked there were about\r\nhalf a million of people in London that didn\xe2\x80\x99t know where the hell the\r\nmorrow\xe2\x80\x99s meal was to come from; what they wanted to do, unless they\r\nwere just a collection of pettifogging old women, was to show them\r\nwhere to get it, to take it to them with heaped-up hands. Hyacinth\r\nlistened, with a divided attention, to interlaced iterations, while the\r\ntalk blew hot and cold; there was a genuine emotion, a quick pulse of\r\nhigh fever, to-night in the rear of the \xe2\x80\x9cSun and Moon,\xe2\x80\x9d and he felt the\r\ncontagion of excited purpose. But he was following a train of his own;\r\nhe was wondering what Muniment had in reserve (for certainly Paul but\r\nplayed with the company), and his imagination, quickened by the sense\r\nof impending relations with the heroic Hoffendahl and the discussion\r\nas to the alternative duty of escaping or of facing one\xe2\x80\x99s fate, had\r\nlaunched itself into possible perils\xe2\x80\x94into the idea of how he might in a\r\ngiven case settle for himself that question of paying for the lot. The\r\nloud, contradictory, vain, unpractical babble went on about him, but\r\nhe was definitely conscious only that the project of breaking into the\r\nbakers\xe2\x80\x99 shops was well before the assembly and was receiving a vigorous\r\ntreatment, and that there was likewise a good deal of reference to\r\nthe butchers and grocers and even to the fishmongers. He was in a\r\nstate of inward exaltation, possessed by an intense desire to stand\r\nface to face with the sublime Hoffendahl, to hear his voice and touch\r\nhis mutilated hand. He was ready for anything: he knew he was himself\r\nsafe to breakfast and dine, if poorly still sufficiently, and that his\r\ncolleagues were perhaps even more crude and clumsy than usual; but a\r\nbreath of popular passion had warmed his cheek and his heart, and he\r\nseemed to see, immensely magnified, the monstrosity of the great ulcers\r\nand sores of London\xe2\x80\x94the sick, eternal misery crying out of the darkness\r\nin vain, confronted with granaries and treasure-houses and places of\r\ndelight where shameless satiety kept guard. In such a mood as this he\r\nfelt there was no need to consider, to reason: the facts themselves\r\nwere as imperative as the cry of the drowning, since while pedantry\r\ngained time didn\xe2\x80\x99t starvation and anguish gain it too? He knew Muniment\r\ndisapproved of delay, that he held the day had come for a forcible\r\nrectification of horrible inequalities. In the last conversation they\r\nhad had together his judicious friend had given him a more definite\r\nwarrant than ever before for numbering him in the party of immediate\r\naction, though indeed he remarked on this occasion, once more, that\r\nthat particular formula the little bookbinder appeared to have taken\r\nsuch a fancy to was mere gibberish. He hated this sort of pretentious\r\nlabel; it was fit only for politicians and amateurs. None the less he\r\nhad been as plain as possible on the point that their game must be now\r\nto frighten society, and frighten it effectually; to make it believe\r\nthat the swindled classes were at last fairly in league\xe2\x80\x94had really\r\ngrasped the idea that, closely combined, they would be irresistible.\r\nThey were not in league and they hadn\xe2\x80\x99t in their totality grasped\r\nany idea at all\xe2\x80\x94Muniment was not slow to make that equally plain.\r\nAll the same society was scareable, and every great scare was a gain\r\nfor the people. If Hyacinth had needed warrant to-night for a faith\r\ntranscending logic he would have found it in his recall of this quiet\r\nprofession; but his friend\xe2\x80\x99s words came back to him mainly to make him\r\nwonder what that friend had in his head just now. He took no part in\r\nany vociferation; he had called Schinkel to come round and sit beside\r\nhim, and the two appeared to confer together in honest ease while the\r\nbrown atmosphere grew denser, the passing to and fro of firebrands more\r\nlively and the flush of faces more portentous. What Hyacinth would\r\nhave liked to know most of all was why Muniment had not mentioned to\r\nhim first that Hoffendahl was in London and that he had seen him;\r\nfor he _had_ seen him, though he had dodged Schinkel\xe2\x80\x99s question\xe2\x80\x94of\r\nthat Hyacinth instantly felt sure. He would ask for more information\r\nlater; and meanwhile he wished, without resentment, but with a patient\r\nconscious ache, that Muniment would treat him with a little more\r\nconfidence. If there were a secret in regard to Hoffendahl\xe2\x80\x94and there\r\nevidently was: Muniment, quite rightly, though he had dropped the\r\nannouncement of his arrival for a certain effect, had no notion of\r\nsharing the rest of what he knew with that raw roomful\xe2\x80\x94if there were\r\nsomething to be silent and devoted about Hyacinth ardently hoped that\r\nto him in particular would a chance be given to show how he could\r\npractise this superiority. He felt hot and nervous; he got up suddenly\r\nand, through the dark tortuous greasy passage communicating with the\r\nouter world, went forth into the street. The air was foul and sleety\r\nbut refreshed him, and he stood in front of the public-house and smoked\r\nanother pipe. Bedraggled figures passed in and out and a damp tattered\r\nwretched man with a spongy purple face, who had been thrust suddenly\r\nacross the threshold, stood and whimpered in the brutal blaze of the\r\nrow of lamps. The puddles glittered roundabout and the silent vista\r\nof the street, bordered with low black houses, stretched away in the\r\nwintry drizzle to right and left, losing itself in the huge tragic city\r\nwhere unmeasured misery lurked beneath the dirty night, ominously,\r\nmonstrously still, only howling, for its pain, in the heated human\r\ncockpit behind him. Ah what could he do? What opportunity would rise?\r\nThe blundering divided counsels he had been listening to but made the\r\nhelplessness of every one concerned more abject. If he had a definite\r\nwish while he stood there it was that that exalted deluded company\r\nshould pour itself forth with Muniment at its head and surge through\r\nthe sleeping world and gather the myriad miserable out of their slums\r\nand burrows, should roll into the selfish squares and lift a tremendous\r\nhungry voice and awaken the gorged indifferent to a terror that would\r\nbring them down. He lingered a quarter of an hour, but this grand\r\ntreat gave no sign of coming off, and he finally returned to the noisy\r\nclub-room in a state of tormented wonder as to what better idea than\r\nthis very bad one (which seemed to our young man to have at the least\r\nthe merit that it was an idea) Muniment could be revolving in that\r\ntoo-comprehensive brain of his.\r\n\r\nAs he re-entered the place he saw the meeting was breaking up in\r\ndisorder, or at all events in confusion, and that certainly no\r\norganised attempt at the rescue of any number of victims would take\r\nplace that night. All the men were on their feet and were turning away\r\namid a shuffle of benches and chairs, a hunch of shabby shoulders,\r\na frugal abatement of flaring gas and a varied vivacity of disgust\r\nand resignation. The moment after Hyacinth came in Mr. Delancey, the\r\nsupposititious hairdresser, jumped upon a chair at the far end of the\r\nroom and shrieked out an accusation which made every one stop and stare\r\nat him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I want you all to know what strikes me before we part company.\r\nThere isn\xe2\x80\x99t a man in the blessed lot of you that isn\xe2\x80\x99t afraid of his\r\nbloody skin\xe2\x80\x94afraid, afraid, afraid! I\xe2\x80\x99ll go anywhere with any one,\r\nbut there isn\xe2\x80\x99t another, by G\xe2\x80\x94, by what I can make out! There isn\xe2\x80\x99t a\r\nmother\xe2\x80\x99s son of you that\xe2\x80\x99ll risk his precious bones!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis little oration affected Hyacinth like a quick blow in the face:\r\nit seemed to leap at him personally, as if a three-legged stool or\r\nsome hideous hob-nailed boot had been shied at him. The room surged\r\nround, heaving up and down, while he was conscious of a loud explosion\r\nof laughter and scorn, of cries of \xe2\x80\x9cOrder, order!\xe2\x80\x9d of some clear word\r\nof Muniment\xe2\x80\x99s, \xe2\x80\x9cI say, Delancey, just step down\xe2\x80\x9d; of Eustache Poupin\r\nshouting out, \xe2\x80\x9c_Vous insultez le peuple\xe2\x80\x94vous insultez le peuple!_\xe2\x80\x9d of\r\nother retorts not remarkable for refinement. The next moment he found\r\nhe had himself sprung up on a chair opposite the barber and that at\r\nthe sight of so prompt a display the commotion had suddenly turned to\r\nalmost amused suspense. It was the first time he had asked the ear of\r\nthe company, which was given on the spot. He was sure he looked very\r\nwhite\xe2\x80\x94it was even possible they could see him tremble. He could only\r\nhope this didn\xe2\x80\x99t make him ridiculous when he said: \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nright of him to say that. There are others besides him. At all events I\r\nwant to speak for myself: it may do some good; I can\xe2\x80\x99t help it. I\xe2\x80\x99m not\r\nafraid; I\xe2\x80\x99m very sure I\xe2\x80\x99m not. I\xe2\x80\x99m ready to do anything that will do\r\nany good; anything, anything\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t care a damned rap. In such a cause\r\nI should like the idea of danger. I don\xe2\x80\x99t consider my bones precious\r\nin the least, compared with some other things. If one\xe2\x80\x99s sure one isn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nafraid, and one\xe2\x80\x99s accused, why shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t one say so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt appeared to him he was talking a long time and when it was over\r\nhe scarcely knew what happened. He felt himself in a moment down\r\nalmost under the feet of the other men; stamped upon with intentions\r\nof applause, of familiarity; laughed over and jeered over, hustled\r\nand poked in the ribs. He felt himself also pressed to the bosom of\r\nEustache Poupin, who apparently was sobbing, while he heard some one\r\nsay, \xe2\x80\x9cDid ye hear the little bloody beggar, as bold as a lion?\xe2\x80\x9d A\r\ntrial of personal prowess between him and Mr. Delancey was proposed,\r\nbut somehow it didn\xe2\x80\x99t take place, and at the end of five minutes the\r\nclub-room had emptied itself, yet clearly not to be reconstituted\r\noutside in a revolutionary procession. Paul Muniment had taken hold of\r\nhim and said, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll trouble you to stay, you small desperado: I\xe2\x80\x99ll be\r\nblowed if I ever expected to see _you_ on the stump!\xe2\x80\x9d Muniment remained\r\nand M. Poupin and Mr. Schinkel lingered, donning overcoats, beneath a\r\ndim surviving gas-burner in the unventilated medium in which at each\r\nrenewed gathering the Bloomsbury club seemed to recognise itself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUpon my life I believe you\xe2\x80\x99re game,\xe2\x80\x9d said Muniment, looking down at\r\nhim with a serious face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course you think it\xe2\x80\x99s swagger, \xe2\x80\x98self-loaf\xe2\x80\x99 as Schinkel says. But it\r\nisn\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d Then Hyacinth asked: \xe2\x80\x9cIn God\xe2\x80\x99s name why don\xe2\x80\x99t we do something?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh my child, to whom do you say it?\xe2\x80\x9d Eustache Poupin exclaimed,\r\nfolding his arms despairingly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho do you mean by \xe2\x80\x98we\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d said Muniment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll the lot of us. There are plenty of them ready.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReady for what? There\xe2\x80\x99s nothing to be done here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHyacinth stared. \xe2\x80\x9cThen why the deuce do you come?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI daresay I shan\xe2\x80\x99t come much more. It\xe2\x80\x99s a place in which you\xe2\x80\x99ve always\r\nseen too much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder if I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen too much in you,\xe2\x80\x9d Hyacinth risked, gazing at his\r\nfriend.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t say that\xe2\x80\x94he\xe2\x80\x99s going to introduce us to Hoffendahl!\xe2\x80\x9d Schinkel\r\nexclaimed, putting away his pipe in a receptacle almost as large as a\r\nfiddle-case.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShould you like to see the right man, Robinson, that is the real\r\nthing?\xe2\x80\x9d Muniment asked with the same rare grave sound.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe real thing?\xe2\x80\x9d Hyacinth looked from one of his companions to the\r\nother.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve never seen it yet\xe2\x80\x94though you think you have.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd why haven\xe2\x80\x99t you shown it me before?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause I had never seen you on the stump.\xe2\x80\x9d This was more lightly said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBother the stump! I was trusting you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly so. That gave me time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t come unless your mind\xe2\x80\x99s made up, _mon petit_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Poupin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you going now\xe2\x80\x94and to see Hoffendahl? Is _he_ the right man?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nHyacinth cried.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t shout it all over the place. He wants a perfect little\r\ngentleman, and if you\xe2\x80\x99re not one\xe2\x80\x94!\xe2\x80\x9d Muniment went on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it true? Are we all going?\xe2\x80\x9d Hyacinth eagerly went on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, these two are in it. They\xe2\x80\x99re not very wise, but they\xe2\x80\x99re decent,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Muniment, looking at Poupin and Schinkel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre _you_ the real thing, Muniment?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Hyacinth, catching this\r\nlook.\r\n\r\nMuniment dropped his eyes on him. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, you\xe2\x80\x99re the lamb of sacrifice he\r\nwants. It\xe2\x80\x99s at the other end of London. We must have a growler.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe calm, my child; _me voici_!\xe2\x80\x9d And Poupin led their young friend out.\r\n\r\nThey all walked away from the \xe2\x80\x9cSun and Moon,\xe2\x80\x9d and it was not for\r\nsome five minutes that they encountered the four-wheeled cab which\r\nso deepened and dignified their purpose. After they were seated in it\r\nHyacinth learned that the \xe2\x80\x9cright man\xe2\x80\x9d was in London but for three days,\r\nwas liable to hurry away on the morrow, and was accustomed to receive\r\nvisits at all kinds of queer hours. It was getting to be midnight; the\r\ndrive seemed interminable to Hyacinth\xe2\x80\x99s impatience and curiosity. He\r\nsat next Muniment, who passed a strong arm round him, holding him all\r\nthe way as if for a tacit sign of indebtedness. This gave Hyacinth\r\npleasure till he began to wonder if it mightn\xe2\x80\x99t represent also the\r\ninstinct to make sure of him as against possible weak afterthoughts.\r\nThey all ended by sitting silent as the cab jogged along murky miles,\r\nand by the time it stopped our young man had wholly lost, in the\r\ndrizzling gloom, a sense of their whereabouts.\r\n\r\n\r\nEND OF VOL.
